Time is a Flowing Entity, Not Celestial Motion
The author proves that the container of events is not celestial motion but a flowing entity, which is time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time must be an independent entity distinct from celestial motion, based on three rational arguments that eliminate the possibility of identifying it with motion or its attributes.

Core Idea
Time is not the motion of the spheres but the container that holds them.

The author presents a rigorous proof by elimination to show that the 'container' in which events occur cannot be celestial motion or any of its attributes. He offers three compelling arguments that demonstrate the absurdity of identifying time with motion. After refuting the alternatives, he establishes time as a distinct flowing entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the structure
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author introduces a 'second division,' indicating this is part of a larger discussion.
The passage is a continuous proof with three distinct arguments.
2
Identify the three arguments
The author presents three arguments: (1) the container-contained impossibility, (2) the absurdity of statements about actions at the stopping of spheres, and (3) the fact that every state of motion itself occurs at a specific time. Find each one.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the container cannot be celestial motion or its attributes, so it must be a flowing entity. Identify what he calls this entity.
The conclusion is 'That is time.'
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about how these arguments refute the idea that time is merely a measure or attribute of motion. Time is an independent entity that serves as the container for all events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني: وهو أن يكون ذلك الشيء صفة من صفات الحركة الفلكية، فهو أظهر فساداً، لأن ذلك الشيء ظرف للحركة الفلكية، والحركة الفلكية جارية مجرى الظرف بجميع صفاتها ونعوتها، فلو كان الظرف شيئاً من صفات تلك الحركة، لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ظرفاً له ومظروفاً له، وهو محال. وأيضاً: فلو قال قائل: إنه تعالى يفعل الفعل الفلاني عند وقوف الأفلاك والكواكب عن حركاتها، لم يكن هذا الكلام كلاماً معلوم الفساد والبطلان في بديهة العقل. فلو كان هذا الظرف شيئاً من الحركات الفلكية أو شيئاً من أحوال تلك الحركات، لكان هذا الكلام مقطوع الفساد في بديهة العقل، لأن حصول الحادث لا في وقت معين: معلوم الامتناع في البديهة. وأيضاً: فلا حال من أحوال الحركة الفلكية، ولا صفة من صفاتها إلا وصريح العقل يحكم بأنها وقعت في الوقت الفلاني في الماضي، أو سيقع في الوقت الفلاني في المستقبل. فثبت بما ذكرنا: أن ذلك الظرف ليس هو الحركة الفلكية، ولا صفة من صفاتها. فثبت: وجود موجود سیال، ليس هو الحركة الفلكية ولا صفة من صفاتها. وذلك هو الزمان.
The second division: and that is that that thing is an attribute of the attributes of celestial motion — it is even more clearly corrupt. Because that thing is a container for celestial motion, and celestial motion runs in the manner of the container with all its attributes and characteristics. So if the container were one of the attributes of that motion, it would necessitate that a single thing, in relation to a single thing, be both container and contained. That is impossible. And also: if someone says: 'Indeed God Most High does such-and-such action at the stopping of the celestial spheres and planets from their motions,' this statement would not be a statement known to be corrupt and invalid in the primary intuition of the intellect. So if this container were one of the celestial motions or one of the states of those motions, this statement would be definitively corrupt in the primary intuition of the intellect; because the occurrence of an event not in a specific time is known to be impossible in the primary intuition. And also: no state of the states of celestial motion, and no attribute of its attributes, except that the pure intellect judges that it occurred at such-and-such time in the past, or will occur at such-and-such time in the future. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that that container is not celestial motion, nor an attribute of its attributes. So it is established: the existence of a flowing entity, which is not celestial motion nor an attribute of its attributes. And that is time.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a proof by elimination. The author argues that the 'container' (الظرف) in which events occur cannot be celestial motion or any of its attributes. He gives three arguments: 1) If the container were an attribute of motion, the same thing would be both container and contained, which is absurd. 2) If the container were motion, then the statement 'God does action X at the stopping of the spheres' would be self-evidently false, but it is not; in fact, an event not occurring in a specific time is intuitively impossible. 3) Every state or attribute of motion is itself located at a specific time, so they cannot be the container that defines time. Therefore, the container must be a distinct flowing entity, which he identifies as time.

Refuting Aristotle on Time: The First Proof
Author introduces the first proof against Aristotle's definition of time as a measure of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against Aristotle's claim that time is a measure of motion, and why motion inherently requires time.

Core Idea
Motion's essence is transition; transition requires time, so time cannot be its external measure.

Having surveyed previous views on time, the author now turns to refute Aristotle's influential definition. He argues that time cannot be a measure of motion because motion itself depends on time for its very nature. This passage lays out the first proof, which uses the idea that motion requires succession, which is time. By understanding this argument, you will see why the relationship between time and motion is more intimate than Aristotle claimed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Begin by reading the entire passage in Arabic and English. Notice that the author states Aristotle's view is false and then presents a proof.
The proof is structured as a dilemma.
2
Understand the two divisions of the proof
The proof splits the need of motion for time into two possibilities: either because it is motion or not. Both are argued to be false, establishing the conclusion.
3
Follow the reasoning that motion requires time by nature
Motion, as transition between states, requires succession (before/after), which is time. This shows motion's essential dependence on time.
This is the key insight: time is intrinsic to motion's nature.
4
Consider the implications for all motions
Since all motions share this dependence equally, time cannot be a measure particular to some motions. The author presents three possibilities, each to be examined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في هذا الباب ولتتكلم الآن في أن الزمان هل يعقل أن يكون مقداراً للحركة الفلكية كما هو قول أرسطاطاليس وأتباعه فنقول هذا المذهب عندنا باطل ويدل عليه وجوه الحجة الأولى إن الحركة الفلكية الأولى لما افتقرت إلى الزمان فذلك الافتقار إما أن يكون لا لأجل كونها حركة وإما أن يكون لأجل كونها حركة والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول يكون الزمان مقداراً للحركة وإنما قلنا إن القسم الأول باطل لأن الحركة من حيث إنها حركة عبارة عن الانتقال من حال إلى حال وإذا كان كذلك فالحركة من حيث إنها حركة لا تقرر ماهيتها ولا تحصل حقيقتها إلا عند حصول تعاقب القبليات والبعديات وهذا المعنى لا يحصل إلا بسبب الزمان فثبت أن الحركة من حيث هي حركة مفتقرة إلى الزمان وإذا ثبت هذا فحينئذ لم يكن حصول الزمان لبعض الحركات ابتداء وللباقي بالتبعية أولى من العكس لأن جهة الاقتضاء لما لم تكن إلا مجرد كونها حركة ثم إن هذا المعنى أمر مشترك فيه بين الكل على السوية كانت جهة الاقتضاء حاصلة في الكل على السوية وعلى هذا التقدير فيمتنع أن يتخصص به بعض الحركات دون البعض وحينئذ إما أن يجب أن يحصل لكل حركة زمان على حدة أو يحصل للكل زمان واحد بعينه أو لا يحصل الزمان لشيء منها
This is the detailed exposition of the schools of people on this topic. And let us now speak about whether time can be understood as a measure of celestial motion as is the saying of Aristotle and his followers. So we say: This school is false according to us. And evidence for it is given by aspects: The first proof: Since the first celestial motion was in need of time, that need is either not because it is motion, or because it is motion; and both divisions are false. So the saying that time is a measure of motion is false. And we only say that the first division is false because motion, insofar as it is motion, is an expression of transition from one state to another. And if that is so, then motion, insofar as it is motion, its quiddity is not established nor its reality obtained except when there is succession of before and after. And this meaning is not obtained except by time. So it is established that motion, insofar as it is motion, is in need of time. And if this is established, then at that time it is not the case that time's occurring for some motions originally and for the rest secondarily is more appropriate than the opposite, because the aspect of requirement was nothing but its being motion. Then this meaning is a matter shared among all equally, so the aspect of requirement obtains in all equally. And on this estimation, it is impossible that it is particularized to some motions without others. And then either each motion must have its own time, or all have one specific time, or time does not occur for any of them.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage concludes the exposition of previous views on time and introduces the author's refutation of Aristotle's definition that time is a measure of celestial motion. It states that this view is false and begins the first proof. The proof argues that motion, by its very nature (as a transition from one state to another), requires the succession of 'before' and 'after,' which is time. Therefore, motion essentially depends on time. Since all motions share this dependence equally, time cannot be a measure particular to some motions; it either belongs to each motion individually, to all equally, or to none. This sets up the three possibilities to be refuted.

Three Possibilities and Refutation of Distinct Times
Author enumerates three possibilities and refutes the first with two reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities for time's relation to motion and why the idea that each motion has its own distinct time is invalid.

Core Idea
Time cannot be split into separate times for each motion without absurdity.

The author begins by setting out a fundamental trilemma about time and motion: either each motion has its own time, all motions share one time, or time is separate from motion altogether. He then launches a devastating critique of the first option, showing that it leads to contradictions and infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the three possibilities
The author lists three exhaustive possibilities. Identify each one.
The three are: distinct times, one shared time, no time for any motion.
2
Understand the first refutation argument
The first argument uses common-sense experience of a single hour. Notice how it appeals to necessary knowledge.
3
Understand the second refutation argument
The second argument deals with co-existing times and the need for an encompassing time, leading to regress or contradiction.
This argument considers both infinite regress and a further contradiction if regress is allowed.
4
Reflect on the overall result
After refuting the first possibility, note that the author also implicitly rejects the possibility of infinite regress raising other issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا التقدير فيمتنع أن يتخصص به بعض الحركات دون البعض ، وحينئذ إما أن يجب أن يحصل لكل حركة زمان على حدة ، أو يحصل للكل زمان واحد بعينه ، أو لا يحصل الزمان لشيء منها . والقسم الأول باطل من وجهين : أحدهما : إن على هذا التقدير يلزم أن لا تكون هذه الساعة الواحدة ساعة واحدة ، بل تكون ساعات كثيرة بعد الحركات الموجودة في العالم الأعلى والعالم الأسفل ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل ، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن هذه الساعة ليست إلا ساعة واحدة . والثاني : إن تلك الأزمنة لما وجدت معاً ، فمعيتها لا بد وأن تكون لزمان آخر محيط بها ، وذلك محال ، لأنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل . وأيضاً : فبتقدير تسليم جواز التسلسل فالمحال لازم، لأن الأمر الذي لاجله حصل اجتماع كل الأزمنة ، لا بد وأن يكون محيطاً بجميعها ، والمحيط بجميع الأزمنة ، وجب أن لا يكون زماناً . لأنا قد حصرنا الأزمنة بأسرها في ذلك المجموع ، فالمحيط بها الخارج عنها ، لا بد وأن يكون زماناً ، لكن قد ثبت أن الشيء الذي يقتضي المعية والقبلية والبعدية هو الزمان ، فذلك الخارج يجب أن يكون زماناً وأن لا يكون زماناً وهذا خلف . محال وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يحصل زمان واحد قائم بجميع الحركات . فهذا أيضاً محال . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن حلول العرض الواحد في المحال الكثيرة محال . والثاني : إنه إذا عدمت حركة ، فقد عدم مقدارها ، والحركة الثانية التي هي باقية موجودة ، يكون مقدارها أيضاً موجوداً ، فلو كان مقدار جميع الحركات مقداراً واحداً بالعدد، لزم كون الشيء الواحد موجوداً معدوماً معاً . وهو محال . والثالث : إن الحركات المختلفة تكون مقاديرها مختلفة . فلو كان مقدار جميع الحركات شيئاً واحداً مع أنها مقادير مختلفة لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد وحده بحسب الشخص تكون له حقائق مختلفة. وذلك محال . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إن الشيء الذي هو زمان الجميع الحركات ، لا يكون عارضاً لشيء من الحركات ، بل يكون مبايناً عنها بأسرها إلا أن الحركات تقع فيها وتتقدر بها . فهذا هو المطلوب. وعند هذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : الزمان عرض قائم بحركة الفلك الأعظم ، بل هو تصريح بأن الزمان غني عن كل الحركات . وهو المطلوب . وهذا الكلام قوي في إثبات هذا المطلوب
On this assumption, it is impossible that it (time) should be particularized to some motions without others. Then either it must be that each motion has its own time separately, or that all motions have one and the same time, or that time does not occur for any of them. The first division is invalid for two reasons: One: on this assumption, it would follow that this single hour is not one hour but many hours according to the motions existing in the upper world and the lower world, and it is known that this is false, for we know by necessity that this hour is only one hour. Second: when those times exist together, their togetherness necessarily requires another time encompassing them, and that is impossible because it leads to an infinite regress. Also, if we grant the possibility of an infinite regress, the impossibility is still necessary, because that for the sake of which all times are gathered must encompass them all, and that which encompasses all times must not be a time, for we have confined all times within that collection. So what encompasses them and is external to them must be a time, but it has been established that that which entails togetherness, priority, and posteriority is time, so that external thing must be time and must not be time, and this is a contradiction. Impossible. As for the second division, namely that there is one time subsisting in all motions, this too is impossible. Evidence for it from several aspects: First, it is impossible for one accident to inhere in many loci. Second, if a motion ceases, its measure ceases, and the second motion which remains exists, its measure also exists. If the measure of all motions were one and the same in number, then the same thing would be existent and non-existent together, which is impossible. Third, different motions have different measures. If the measure of all motions were one thing while they have different measures, then one and the same individual thing would have different realities, which is impossible. As for the third division, that it be said that the thing which is the time of all motions is not an accident of any motion, but is separate from all of them, except that motions occur in it and are measured by it – this is the desired conclusion. At this point the falsity of the statement of those who say that time is an accident subsisting in the motion of the outermost sphere becomes apparent; rather, it is a declaration that time is independent of all motions. And that is the desired conclusion. And this speech is strong in establishing this claim.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof that time is not an accident of motion but an independent entity in which motions occur. The author sets out three logical possibilities for the relationship between time and motion: either (1) each motion has its own distinct time, (2) all motions share one time that inheres in them, or (3) time does not attach to any motion but is a separate container. He refutes the first possibility with two arguments. First, if each motion had its own time, then a single hour would become many hours according to the number of motions, but we know necessarily that this hour is only one. Second, if these times co-exist, they require an encompassing time, leading to an infinite regress; even if one accepts infinite regress, the encompassing entity must be time and not time simultaneously, a contradiction. He then refutes the second possibility with three arguments: (i) one accident cannot inhere in multiple loci, (ii) if the measure of all motions were one, cessation of one motion would make that measure both existent and non-existent, and (iii) different motions have different measures, so a single measure cannot have multiple realities. The third possibility remains: time is separate from all motions and they occur within it and are measured by it. This is the correct view, and it directly refutes the claim that time is an accident of the outermost sphere's motion. The author concludes that this argument is strong.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is particularized to each motion, so each motion has its own distinct time.
Correction: This leads to contradictions: a single hour would be many hours, and co-existing times require an encompassing time, causing regress or contradiction.

Checkpoint

C) It violates the principle of non-contradiction.
D) It implies time is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first possibility, that each motion has its own time, is impossible because it conflicts with our necessary knowledge and leads to infinite regress or contradiction.

?

Refutation of Shared Time and Proof of Time as Independent
Author refutes the second possibility and proves the third, independent, view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that time is a single accident subsisting in all motions is false, and how the correct view establishes time as an independent entity.

Core Idea
Time cannot be a single accident shared by all motions; it must be a separate container in which motions occur.

Having refuted the first possibility, the author now turns to the second: that all motions share one and the same time that inheres in them. He provides three compelling arguments against this, then affirms the third possibility as the correct view, in which time is independent of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the second possibility
The author gives three reasons. Identify each one.
2
Understand the first argument
One accident cannot inhere in many loci. Think about why this is a fundamental principle.
3
Understand the second argument
If a motion ceases, its measure ceases; but if all motions share one measure, that measure would be both existent and non-existent.
4
Understand the third argument
Different motions have different measures, so one measure cannot have multiple realities.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The third possibility is affirmed: time is not an accident of any motion but is separate, and motions occur within it.
This directly refutes the claim that time is an accident of the motion of the outermost sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا التقدير فيمتنع أن يتخصص به بعض الحركات دون البعض ، وحينئذ إما أن يجب أن يحصل لكل حركة زمان على حدة ، أو يحصل للكل زمان واحد بعينه ، أو لا يحصل الزمان لشيء منها . والقسم الأول باطل من وجهين : أحدهما : إن على هذا التقدير يلزم أن لا تكون هذه الساعة الواحدة ساعة واحدة ، بل تكون ساعات كثيرة بعد الحركات الموجودة في العالم الأعلى والعالم الأسفل ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل ، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن هذه الساعة ليست إلا ساعة واحدة . والثاني : إن تلك الأزمنة لما وجدت معاً ، فمعيتها لا بد وأن تكون لزمان آخر محيط بها ، وذلك محال ، لأنه يؤدي إلى التسلسل . وأيضاً : فبتقدير تسليم جواز التسلسل فالمحال لازم، لأن الأمر الذي لاجله حصل اجتماع كل الأزمنة ، لا بد وأن يكون محيطاً بجميعها ، والمحيط بجميع الأزمنة ، وجب أن لا يكون زماناً . لأنا قد حصرنا الأزمنة بأسرها في ذلك المجموع ، فالمحيط بها الخارج عنها ، لا بد وأن يكون زماناً ، لكن قد ثبت أن الشيء الذي يقتضي المعية والقبلية والبعدية هو الزمان ، فذلك الخارج يجب أن يكون زماناً وأن لا يكون زماناً وهذا خلف . محال وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يحصل زمان واحد قائم بجميع الحركات . فهذا أيضاً محال . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن حلول العرض الواحد في المحال الكثيرة محال . والثاني : إنه إذا عدمت حركة ، فقد عدم مقدارها ، والحركة الثانية التي هي باقية موجودة ، يكون مقدارها أيضاً موجوداً ، فلو كان مقدار جميع الحركات مقداراً واحداً بالعدد، لزم كون الشيء الواحد موجوداً معدوماً معاً . وهو محال . والثالث : إن الحركات المختلفة تكون مقاديرها مختلفة . فلو كان مقدار جميع الحركات شيئاً واحداً مع أنها مقادير مختلفة لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد وحده بحسب الشخص تكون له حقائق مختلفة. وذلك محال . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إن الشيء الذي هو زمان الجميع الحركات ، لا يكون عارضاً لشيء من الحركات ، بل يكون مبايناً عنها بأسرها إلا أن الحركات تقع فيها وتتقدر بها . فهذا هو المطلوب. وعند هذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : الزمان عرض قائم بحركة الفلك الأعظم ، بل هو تصريح بأن الزمان غني عن كل الحركات . وهو المطلوب . وهذا الكلام قوي في إثبات هذا المطلوب
On this assumption, it is impossible that it (time) should be particularized to some motions without others. Then either it must be that each motion has its own time separately, or that all motions have one and the same time, or that time does not occur for any of them. The first division is invalid for two reasons: One: on this assumption, it would follow that this single hour is not one hour but many hours according to the motions existing in the upper world and the lower world, and it is known that this is false, for we know by necessity that this hour is only one hour. Second: when those times exist together, their togetherness necessarily requires another time encompassing them, and that is impossible because it leads to an infinite regress. Also, if we grant the possibility of an infinite regress, the impossibility is still necessary, because that for the sake of which all times are gathered must encompass them all, and that which encompasses all times must not be a time, for we have confined all times within that collection. So what encompasses them and is external to them must be a time, but it has been established that that which entails togetherness, priority, and posteriority is time, so that external thing must be time and must not be time, and this is a contradiction. Impossible. As for the second division, namely that there is one time subsisting in all motions, this too is impossible. Evidence for it from several aspects: First, it is impossible for one accident to inhere in many loci. Second, if a motion ceases, its measure ceases, and the second motion which remains exists, its measure also exists. If the measure of all motions were one and the same in number, then the same thing would be existent and non-existent together, which is impossible. Third, different motions have different measures. If the measure of all motions were one thing while they have different measures, then one and the same individual thing would have different realities, which is impossible. As for the third division, that it be said that the thing which is the time of all motions is not an accident of any motion, but is separate from all of them, except that motions occur in it and are measured by it – this is the desired conclusion. At this point the falsity of the statement of those who say that time is an accident subsisting in the motion of the outermost sphere becomes apparent; rather, it is a declaration that time is independent of all motions. And that is the desired conclusion. And this speech is strong in establishing this claim.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof that time is not an accident of motion but an independent entity in which motions occur. The author sets out three logical possibilities for the relationship between time and motion: either (1) each motion has its own distinct time, (2) all motions share one time that inheres in them, or (3) time does not attach to any motion but is a separate container. He refutes the first possibility with two arguments. First, if each motion had its own time, then a single hour would become many hours according to the number of motions, but we know necessarily that this hour is only one. Second, if these times co-exist, they require an encompassing time, leading to an infinite regress; even if one accepts infinite regress, the encompassing entity must be time and not time simultaneously, a contradiction. He then refutes the second possibility with three arguments: (i) one accident cannot inhere in multiple loci, (ii) if the measure of all motions were one, cessation of one motion would make that measure both existent and non-existent, and (iii) different motions have different measures, so a single measure cannot have multiple realities. The third possibility remains: time is separate from all motions and they occur within it and are measured by it. This is the correct view, and it directly refutes the claim that time is an accident of the outermost sphere's motion. The author concludes that this argument is strong.

تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a single accident that subsists in all motions collectively.
Correction: This is impossible because one accident cannot inhere in many loci, and because it leads to contradictions regarding existence and non-existence and differing measures.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given to refute the idea that all motions share one time?
A) One accident cannot inhere in many loci.
B) It leads to an infinite regress.
C) Time would be eternal.
D) It contradicts the definition of motion.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that time is independent of motion, a separate entity in which motions occur, not an accident of any motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living as if your actions create time, or as if time is a gift in which your actions are recorded? How will you change your relationship with time after this lesson?
?
The author argues that time cannot be owned by any motion or person. Reflect on a time you felt you 'owned' your time. The Third Proof: Time Cannot Be a Single Quantity
Third argument: impossibility of time as a single quantity for all motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational proof against the notion that time is a single quantity underlying all motions.

Core Idea
Different motions have different quantities; one quantity cannot fit all.

The author continues his investigation into the nature of time by presenting a third argument. This argument targets the idea that time could be a single measure for all motions, showing that this leads to logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the third proof
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'مقداراً واحداً' up to 'وذلك محال'. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the author presents a hypothetical and then shows its impossibility.
The proof is based on the diversity of motions and their measures.
2
Understand the logical contradiction
The proof argues that if all motions had the same quantity, then a single thing would have opposite attributes—existent and non-existent together. Also, different motions would require different realities for one thing. Grasp why these are impossible.
3
Connect to the broader discussion
This proof is one of several arguments against time being an accident of motion. It sets the stage for the positive view of time as an independent entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقداراً واحداً بالعدد، لزم كون الشيء الواحد موجوداً معدوماً معاً . وهو محال . والثالث : إن الحركات المختلفة تكون مقاديرها مختلفة . فلو كان مقدار جميع الحركات شيئاً واحداً مع أنها مقادير مختلفة لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد وحده بحسب الشخص . تكون له حقائق مختلفة. وذلك محال . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : إن الشيء الذي هو زمان الجميع الحركات ، لا يكون عارضاً لشيء من الحركات ، بل يكون مبايناً عنها بأسرها إلا أن الحركات تقع فيها وتتقدر بها . فهذا هو المطلوب. وعند هذا يظهر فساد قول من يقول : الزمان عرض قائم بحركة الفلك الأعظم ، بل هو تصريح بأن الزمان غني عن كل الحركات . وهو المطلوب . وهذا الكلام قوي في إثبات هذا المطلوب.
A single quantity in number would necessitate the one thing being existent and non-existent together. That is impossible. The third [proof]: different motions have different quantities. So if the quantity of all motions were one thing while they are different quantities, it would necessitate that the one thing by itself, according to the individual, would have different realities. That is impossible. As for the third division: it is that one says: the thing which is the time of all motions is not an accident of any motion, but rather is distinct from all of them, except that motions occur in it and are measured by it. This is what is sought. And here appears the falsity of the statement of those who say: time is an accident subsisting in the motion of the outermost sphere. Rather, it is a declaration that time is independent of all motions. And that is what is sought. And this speech is strong in establishing this sought [truth].
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents part of a proof concerning the nature of time. The author first offers a third argument against the view that time is a single quantity for all motions: if different motions have different magnitudes, then assigning them all one measure would lead to a contradiction (the same thing having different realities). He then introduces a third division of possible views: time is not an accident of any specific motion but is distinct from all motions, and yet motions occur in it and are measured by it. This directly refutes the claim that time is an accident of the celestial sphere's motion, and instead affirms that time is independent of all motions. The author asserts the strength of this argument.

Correction: The argument shows that different motions have different quantities, so a single measure would lead to logical absurdities.

Key Takeaway

The Third Division: Time Is Distinct from All Motions
Third division: time as independent of motions, refuting the accident view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division (القسم الثالث) which affirms that time is not an accident of any motion but is independent, and motions are measured by it.

Core Idea
Time is not an accident of motion; it is a distinct container for motions.

After refuting other views, the author presents his own positive position: time is not an accident subsisting in any motion, but rather a distinct entity in which motions occur and by which they are measured. This directly opposes the prevalent view that time is an accident of the celestial sphere's motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third division
Read from 'وأما القسم الثالث' to the end. Focus on the claim and the refutation of the opposing view.
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that time is not an accident of any motion but is distinct (مباين) from all motions. Yet motions occur 'in' time and are measured by it.
This is the desired position (المطلوب).
3
Notice the refutation
The author explicitly declares that the statement 'time is an accident of the outermost sphere's motion' is false. Instead, time is independent of all motions.
The argument is presented as strong (قوي).

Ibn Sina's Answer and Its Summary
Ibn Sina's verbatim response from al-Shifa and the author's summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's distinctions between time as a measure and time as an accident, and why the author considers his answer insufficient.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's attempt to tie time to one motion fails because all motions equally require time.

Ibn Sina tries to defend the possibility of a beginningless time by distinguishing different ways time can relate to motion. He argues that time can be the measure of all motions without being an accident of each. Let's examine his verbatim answer from al-Shifa and the author's critical summary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get an overview of Ibn Sina's distinctions and the author's summary.
Notice the three distinctions he makes.
2
Identify the three key distinctions
Ibn Sina distinguishes: (1) time as measure vs. essence of time, (2) time as accident of motion vs. motion as accident of time, and (3) the condition for something to measure something else.
These are central to his argument.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's conclusion
He argues that time can be primarily related to one continuous motion and secondarily measure others, like a measure in a body measures that body and also what is parallel.
This tries to avoid the need for every motion to have its own time.
4
Reflect on the author's summary
The author reduces Ibn Sina's lengthy argument to a single point: time is the measure of a specific motion, and others are measured by it. Consider why the author finds this unsatisfactory.
The author's refined proof will later show the flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس ذكر هذه الحجة في الشفاء لا على هذا الوجه التام في التقرير والتحرير لكن ذكرها ناقصة مشوشة . ثم إنه أجاب عنها في فصل آخر ونحن ننقل جوابه بلفظه ، ثم ننظر فيه على سبيل الإنصاف : أنه هل يكفي في دفع هذا الكلام أم لا ؟ قال : في كتاب الشفاء : « والذي قيل : أنه إن كان للزمان وجود، وجب أن تتبع كل حركة زمان فتكون كل حركة تستتبع زماناً . فالجواب عن ذلك : أنه فرق بين أن يقال: إن الزمان مقدار لكل حركة، وبين أن يقال : إن ذات الزمان البته متعلقة بكل حركة، وأيضاً فرق بين أن يقال : إن ذات الزمان متعلقة بالحركة على سبيل العروض لها، وبين أن يقال : إن ذات الحركة متعلق بها الزمان على سبيل أن الزمان يعرض لها. أما الأول فلانه ليس من شرط ما تقدر الشيء أن يكون عارضاً له وقائماً به ، بل ربما قدر المباين بالموافاة والموازاة لما هو مباين له ، وأما الثاني فلأنه ليس أمر إذا تعلق ذات شيء بطبيعة شيء ، يجب أن لا تخلو طبيعة الشيء عنه. ونحن إنما تبرهن لنا من أمر الزمان أنه متعلق الحركة وهيئة لها. ومن أمر الحركة : أن كل حركة فإنها متقدرة بزمان ، وليس يلزم من هذين أن تكون كل حركة ، فإنه يتعلق بها زمان يخصها ، ولا أن كل ما قدر شيئاً فهو عارض له ، حتى يكون لكل حركة زمان عارض لذاتها بعينه ، بل الحركات التي لها ابتداء وانتهاء لا يتعلق بها الزمان . نعم إن وجد الزمان الحركة على صفة يصلح أن يتعلق بها وجود الزمان تقدر به سائر الحركات ، وهذه الحركة يجب أن تكون حركة يصح عليها الاستمرار ، ولا يحصل لها بالفعل أطراف ، ثم إن الشيخ الرئيس أطنب في الكلام وطول إلى أن قال : « فالزمان وجوده متعلق بحركة واحدة يقدرها ، ويقدر أيضاً سائر الحركات، وذلك كالمقدار الموجود في جسم ، فإنه يقدره ويقدر أيضاً ما يحاذيه ويوازيه » ، هذا نص كلامه . وأقول : حاصل هذا التطويل يرجع إلى حرف واحد ، وهو أنه : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الزمان عبارة عن مقدار حركة معينة ، ثم إن تلك الحركة تتقدر بذلك المقدار ابتداء ، ثم بواسطة تقدر تلك الحركة بذلك المقدار تتقدر أيضاً سائر الحركات بذلك الزمان ؟ فهذا هو الحاصل من هذه الكلمات الطويلة . واعلم أنا حررنا ذلك الدليل بحيث لا يتوجه عليه هذا السؤال ، وذلك لأنا بينا أن افتقار الحركة إلى الزمان ، ليس لكونها حركة مخصوصة ، بل لكونها حركة فقط . وهذا المعنى أمر مشترك فيه بين جميع الحركات . وحينئذ يلزم أن يقال : إن جهة الاقتضاء والافتقار حاصلة في جميع الحركات على السوية من غير
And know that the Chief Master mentioned this argument in al-Shifa not in this complete manner in formulation and exposition, but he mentioned it deficient and confused. Then he answered it in another chapter, and we transmit his answer verbatim, then we examine it in a fair manner: whether it suffices to refute this speech or not? He said in the book al-Shifa: 'What was said: if time has existence, then every motion must follow a time, so every motion entails a time. The answer to that is: there is a difference between saying that time is the measure of every motion, and between saying that the essence of time is absolutely related to every motion. Also there is a difference between saying that the essence of time is related to motion as an accident in it, and between saying that the essence of motion is such that time is related to it as an accident in it. As for the first, because it is not a condition of whatever measures a thing that it be an accident inhering in it; rather, perhaps the distinct [thing] may measure by correspondence and parallelism to what is distinct from it. As for the second, because it is not the case that if the essence of something is related to the nature of a thing, that the nature of the thing must not be without it. And we have demonstrated concerning time that it is related to motion and is a state of it. And concerning motion: that every motion is measured by time. But from these two it does not necessarily follow that every motion has a time particular to it, nor that everything that measures something is an accident in it, such that every motion has a time that is an accident in its very essence. Rather, motions that have a beginning and an end are not related to time. Rather, if time finds the motion in a state suitable to be related to it, then the existence of time is [such that] by it all other motions are measured. And this motion must be a motion that can continue and does not have actual ends. Then the Chief Master elaborated and lengthened [the speech] until he said: 'So the existence of time is related to a single motion that it measures, and it also measures other motions, like the measure existing in a body, for it measures it and also measures what is parallel and corresponding to it.' This is the text of his speech. I say: the outcome of this long-windedness reduces to a single point: namely, why it is not permissible to say: time is the measure of a specific motion, then that motion is measured by that measure initially, then by means of that motion being measured by that measure, other motions are also measured by that time? This is the outcome of these lengthy words. And know that we have refined that argument such that this question does not apply to it, because we have shown that the need of motion for time is not because it is a specific motion, but because it is motion simply. And this meaning is common to all motions. Then it follows that the aspect of requirement and need obtains in all motions equally, without [distinction].
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) response to the argument and the author's critique. The author first notes that Ibn Sina's presentation of the argument in al-Shifa was incomplete and confused, and then quotes his answer verbatim. Ibn Sina distinguishes between different ways time can relate to motion: as a measure, or as an essence, or as an accident. He argues that time can be a measure of all motions without being an accident of each, and that it is possible for time to be related primarily to one continuous motion and secondarily measure others. The author then summarizes Ibn Sina's answer as essentially saying that time is the measure of a specific motion and then other motions are measured by it. The author rejects this by presenting his own refined proof: the need of motion for time is due to the very nature of motion, not to any particular motion. Since all motions share this need equally, time cannot be tied to one specific motion. Thus the author's argument is immune to Ibn Sina's objection.

The Author's Refined Proof for Time's Universality
The author's refined proof that time is necessary for all motions equally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author's refined proof shows that time cannot be derived from any particular motion, because all motions require time equally.

Core Idea
All motions need time because they are motion, so time must be independent and not tied to one motion.

After summarizing Ibn Sina's answer, the author presents his own refined argument that avoids the objection. He argues that the need for time is based on the very nature of motion, not on specific characteristics. Since all motions share this need, time cannot be limited to a single motion. Let's study this concise but powerful proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the refined proof
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The author states that the need of motion for time is because it is motion, not because it is a specific type of motion.
This is the key premise.
2
Understand the implication
Since all motions share the property of being motion, they all equally require time. Therefore time cannot be dependent on any one motion; it must be a separate entity that relates to all motions.
This refutes Ibn Sina's idea of time being primarily tied to one motion.
3
Compare with Ibn Sina's answer
Notice how the author's argument directly addresses the flaw in Ibn Sina's view. If time were tied to one motion, that motion would have no special claim to time over others.
The author has 'refined' the earlier argument to be immune to the objection.

Time Cannot Be Exclusive to a Single Motion
The author refutes Ibn Sina's claim that time attaches to a single motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina's attempt to make time the measure of a specific motion fails, because all motions equally need time.

Core Idea
All motions need time equally, so time cannot belong to any one alone.

Ibn Sina argued that time is the measure of a single continuous motion, and other motions are measured by it indirectly. The author counters that motion's need for time is inherent to motion itself—common to all motions—so it is impossible for time to be exclusive to one. This lesson walks through that essential critique.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's summarized position
Read the author's summary of Ibn Sina's argument that time is related to a single motion that measures all others.
2
Identify the reformulation
Notice how the author reformulates the proof: motion needs time not because it is a specific motion but simply because it is motion.
This is key to the critique.
3
Understand the conclusion
Follow the logic that since all motions share the same need for time, time cannot be assigned to some and not others.

مفهوم ملخص معين ، وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المفهوم من امتداد وجود الشيء بقاؤه واستمراره ودوامه . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : من المعلوم بالضرورة أن دوام وجود الشيء يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً مبايناً عنه ، حاصلا في ، وأنه يمتنع أن يحصل هناك مقدار واحد يكون قائماً بواحد منها ، وتكون سائر الحركات في أنفها خالية عن مقاديرها . أما قول الشيخ : إن المقدار الموجود في جسم يقدره ثم تنقدر سائر الأجسام بواسطته ، فنقول : هذا مغالطة محضة
It is distinct from it. As for the second, it is because it is not a matter that if the essence of something is related to the nature of something, the nature of that thing must not be devoid of it. And we have only proven from the matter of time that it is related to motion and is a state of it. And from the matter of motion: that every motion is measured by time, but it does not follow from these two that every motion is such that a time particular to it is attached, nor that everything that measures something is an accident of it, such that for every motion there is a time accidental to its very essence. Rather, motions that have a beginning and an end are not attached to time. Indeed, if time finds motion in a state suitable for time to attach to it, then all motions are measured by it. And this motion must be a motion that admits continuity and has no termini in actuality. Then the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) went on at length in speech and prolonged until he said: 'Time, its existence is related to a single motion that measures it, and it also measures all other motions, just like the magnitude present in a body, for it measures it and also measures what is parallel and corresponding to it.' This is the text of his words. And I say: The gist of this prolixity reduces to a single point, namely: why may it not be said that time is an expression of the measure of a particular motion, then that motion is measured by that measure in the first place, then by means of that motion being measured by that measure, all other motions are also measured by that time? This is the gist of these lengthy words. And know that we have reformulated that proof in such a way that this question does not apply to it, because we showed that motion's need for time is not because it is a particular motion, but only because it is motion. And this meaning is common to all motions. Then it follows that the aspect of necessitation and need is present in all motions equally without any difference at all. And when this is the case, it is impossible to say that that time occurs for some of those motions to the exclusion of others. And know that our purpose in quoting the Shaykh's words is to show that we only reformulated that proof in a way that the Shaykh's words do not affect it and do not impugn it. Then, after drawing attention to this objective, we show that this speech he mentioned is flawed from another angle. Its formulation: The intelligible of the measure of motion is the measure of the extension of motion, because if we do not interpret the measure of motion in this sense, no definite, summarized concept emerges from our saying 'the measure of motion'. And if you know this, we say: The concept of the extension of the existence of a thing is its persistence, continuity, and duration. If this is established, we say: It is known by necessity that the duration of the existence of a thing is impossible to be an entity distinct from it, existing in something else. That is because people disagreed about whether the persistence and continuity of a thing is an additional attribute to its existence or not. Some denied it is an additional attribute, and some affirmed it. And everyone who said that said that that attribute is present in that thing, and it is impossible for it to be present in something else. And among rational people there is not one who would allow saying that the duration of a thing's existence is not present in it but in something else. And if it is established that the duration of a thing's existence must be in it and not in something else, then the measure of this duration is nothing but a modality of these modalities of this duration, so this modality is, by the impossibility of being distinct, more appropriate. So it is established by what we have summarized: that whenever we interpret time as the measure of motion, the intuitive intellect judges that the measure of every motion must be subsisting in that motion, and it is impossible for there to be a single measure subsisting in one of them while all other motions are in themselves devoid of their measures. As for the Shaykh's statement: 'the magnitude present in a body measures it, then all other bodies are measured by its mediation', we say: this is pure sophistry.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a sustained critique of Ibn Sina's (the Chief Master) account of time as the measure of a single motion that serves as a standard for all motions. The author first summarizes Ibn Sina's lengthy argument and reduces it to a single point: time is the measure of a specific motion, and other motions are measured by time only through the mediation of that motion. The author objects by pointing out that he has already reformulated the proof of time's necessity for motion in a way that avoids this objection: motion's need for time is not due to any particular motion but to motion qua motion, which is common to all motions. Therefore, the aspect of need is equal across all motions, and time cannot be exclusive to some. The author then clarifies that his quotation of Ibn Sina was only to show that his own refined proof is immune to Ibn Sina's objection. Subsequently, he launches a deeper critique: the concept of 'measure of motion' must be understood as the extension of motion, i.e., its duration. Duration of a thing's existence must inhere in that thing itself, not in a separate entity. Even those who affirm persistence as an additional attribute hold that it exists in the thing, not elsewhere. Hence, the measure of duration is a modality of that duration and cannot be separated. Consequently, if time is the measure of motion, each motion must have its own measure subsisting in it; a single measure for all motions is impossible. Finally, the author dismisses Ibn Sina's analogy of a body's magnitude measuring other bodies as pure sophistry, implying it fails to prove the point.

المقدار

Misconception: One might think that if time is the measure of a single motion, other motions are directly measured by that time without needing their own relation to time.
Correction: The author argues that all motions equally need time, so time cannot be exclusive—meaning other motions must also be directly related to time, not merely through another motion.

The Measure of Motion Must Inhere in Its Own Motion
The author argues that duration must inhere in the thing, so each motion has its own measure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the duration of a thing must inhere in itself, implying that each motion has its own measure of time.

Core Idea
Duration must be in the thing itself, so each motion has its own measure.

The author deepens the critique by analyzing the concept of 'measure of motion.' He argues that the measure of motion is its extension, i.e., its duration. Since duration must inhere in the thing whose duration it is, each motion must have its own measure subsisting in it. Thus, a single time for all motions is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of measure of motion
Read the author's explanation that the measure of motion is the extension of motion, i.e., its persistence and duration.
2
Grasp the necessary inherence of duration
The author argues that duration of a thing cannot exist separately from that thing; it must be in it. This is known by necessity.
Even those who affirm persistence as an attribute say it inheres in the thing.
3
Apply to time as measure
If time is the measure of motion, then each motion's measure must be in that motion. A single measure for all motions is impossible.
4
Refute Ibn Sina's analogy
The author dismisses Ibn Sina's analogy of a body's magnitude measuring other bodies as pure sophistry.
The analogy fails because magnitude can be in one body and measure others by comparison, but duration cannot.

هذا مغالطة محضة
It is distinct from it. As for the second, it is because it is not a matter that if the essence of something is related to the nature of something, the nature of that thing must not be devoid of it. And we have only proven from the matter of time that it is related to motion and is a state of it. And from the matter of motion: that every motion is measured by time, but it does not follow from these two that every motion is such that a time particular to it is attached, nor that everything that measures something is an accident of it, such that for every motion there is a time accidental to its very essence. Rather, motions that have a beginning and an end are not attached to time. Indeed, if time finds motion in a state suitable for time to attach to it, then all motions are measured by it. And this motion must be a motion that admits continuity and has no termini in actuality. Then the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) went on at length in speech and prolonged until he said: 'Time, its existence is related to a single motion that measures it, and it also measures all other motions, just like the magnitude present in a body, for it measures it and also measures what is parallel and corresponding to it.' This is the text of his words. And I say: The gist of this prolixity reduces to a single point, namely: why may it not be said that time is an expression of the measure of a particular motion, then that motion is measured by that measure in the first place, then by means of that motion being measured by that measure, all other motions are also measured by that time? This is the gist of these lengthy words. And know that we have reformulated that proof in such a way that this question does not apply to it, because we showed that motion's need for time is not because it is a particular motion, but only because it is motion. And this meaning is common to all motions. Then it follows that the aspect of necessitation and need is present in all motions equally without any difference at all. And when this is the case, it is impossible to say that that time occurs for some of those motions to the exclusion of others. And know that our purpose in quoting the Shaykh's words is to show that we only reformulated that proof in a way that the Shaykh's words do not affect it and do not impugn it. Then, after drawing attention to this objective, we show that this speech he mentioned is flawed from another angle. Its formulation: The intelligible of the measure of motion is the measure of the extension of motion, because if we do not interpret the measure of motion in this sense, no definite, summarized concept emerges from our saying 'the measure of motion'. And if you know this, we say: The concept of the extension of the existence of a thing is its persistence, continuity, and duration. If this is established, we say: It is known by necessity that the duration of the existence of a thing is impossible to be an entity distinct from it, existing in something else. That is because people disagreed about whether the persistence and continuity of a thing is an additional attribute to its existence or not. Some denied it is an additional attribute, and some affirmed it. And everyone who said that said that that attribute is present in that thing, and it is impossible for it to be present in something else. And among rational people there is not one who would allow saying that the duration of a thing's existence is not present in it but in something else. And if it is established that the duration of a thing's existence must be in it and not in something else, then the measure of this duration is nothing but a modality of these modalities of this duration, so this modality is, by the impossibility of being distinct, more appropriate. So it is established by what we have summarized: that whenever we interpret time as the measure of motion, the intuitive intellect judges that the measure of every motion must be subsisting in that motion, and it is impossible for there to be a single measure subsisting in one of them while all other motions are in themselves devoid of their measures. As for the Shaykh's statement: 'the magnitude present in a body measures it, then all other bodies are measured by its mediation', we say: this is pure sophistry.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a sustained critique of Ibn Sina's (the Chief Master) account of time as the measure of a single motion that serves as a standard for all motions. The author first summarizes Ibn Sina's lengthy argument and reduces it to a single point: time is the measure of a specific motion, and other motions are measured by time only through the mediation of that motion. The author objects by pointing out that he has already reformulated the proof of time's necessity for motion in a way that avoids this objection: motion's need for time is not due to any particular motion but to motion qua motion, which is common to all motions. Therefore, the aspect of need is equal across all motions, and time cannot be exclusive to some. The author then clarifies that his quotation of Ibn Sina was only to show that his own refined proof is immune to Ibn Sina's objection. Subsequently, he launches a deeper critique: the concept of 'measure of motion' must be understood as the extension of motion, i.e., its duration. Duration of a thing's existence must inhere in that thing itself, not in a separate entity. Even those who affirm persistence as an additional attribute hold that it exists in the thing, not elsewhere. Hence, the measure of duration is a modality of that duration and cannot be separated. Consequently, if time is the measure of motion, each motion must have its own measure subsisting in it; a single measure for all motions is impossible. Finally, the author dismisses Ibn Sina's analogy of a body's magnitude measuring other bodies as pure sophistry, implying it fails to prove the point.

Second Argument: Time Cannot Be the Measure of Motion
Second proof that time is not the measure of motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against time being the measure of motion, specifically why the extension of motion has no external existence and how this leads to the conclusion that time cannot be identified with the measure of motion.

Core Idea
The extension of motion does not exist externally, so its measure cannot be real.

This passage presents a detailed argument that time cannot be identified with the measure of motion because motion's extension is not a real existent in the external world. The argument carefully distinguishes between the discrete instants that actually occur and the continuous extension we conceive, leading to a key conclusion in the philosophy of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author is arguing that time is not the measure of motion.
The passage starts by assuming the definition of time as the quantity of the extension of motion.
2
Understand why the extension of motion has no external existence
Focus on the part where the author explains that motion consists of successive occurrences at consecutive positions, and only one occurrence exists at any instant. Hence, the totality (extension) never exists simultaneously.
3
Examine the objection and the response
The objector claims motion is a continuous state. Note how the author responds by asserting only each discrete position exists at each instant.
4
Follow the reasoning that the quantity cannot exist if the extension does not
The author argues that the quantity (measure) is a property of the extension. If the extension does not exist externally, its property is even more impossible.
5
See how this leads to the conclusion
Since the Shaykh proved time exists externally, and the measure of motion does not, time cannot be that measure. Reflect on the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه مقدار امتداد الحركة. إذا عرفت هذا. فنقول : امتداد الحركة لا وجود له في الأعيان ، وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون مقدار ذلك الامتداد أمراً موجوداً في الأعيان ، أما بيان أن امتداد الحركة لا وجود له في الأعيان فهو أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز معاقبة ، ومن المعلوم بالبديهة أنه لا وجود لمجموع تلك الحصولات في الأعيان، وإنما الموجود منها حصول واحد في آن واحد ، ثم ينقضي ويحصل بعده مثله . فثبت : أن امتداد الحركة لا وجود له في الأعيان. فإن قالوا : الحركة عبارة عن كون الجسم متوسطاً بين المبدأ والمنتهى ، بحيث لا يكون حاله في آن من الآنات المفروضة في تلك المدة مشابهاً لحاله قبل ذلك ولا بعده ، وهذه الحالة باقية مستمرة . وذلك يدل على أن امتداد الحركة أمر موجود في الأعيان فنقول : الذي يحصل في الأعيان هو الحصول في هذا المعين ، ثم في حد آخر معين . وهكذا على التعاقب والتوالي ، وذلك يدل على أن امتداد الحركة لا حصول له في الأعيان، وأما بيان أنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون لمقدار ذلك الامتداد وجود في الأعيان ، فالدليل عليه : ان مقدار هذا الامتداد صفة من صفات هذا الامتداد ، والموصوف إذا كان ممتنع الوجود في الأعيان، فالصفة أولى بأن تكون ممتنعة الوجود في الأعيان ، فثبت بهذا الذي بيناه : أن مقدار الحركة يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً في الأعيان . لكن الشيخ بين بالدلائل : أن الزمان موجود في الأعيان . ينتج أنه لا شيء من الزمان عبارة عن مقدار الحركة .
It is the quantity of the extension of motion. If you know this, then we say: The extension of motion has no existence in external things (al-aʿyān), and if that is the case, it is impossible that the quantity of that extension be something existing in external things. As for the demonstration that the extension of motion has no existence in external things: it is that motion consists of successive occurrences in consecutive positions, and it is self-evident that the totality of those occurrences has no existence in external things; rather, what exists is a single occurrence at one instant, then it passes away and a similar one occurs after it. So it is established that the extension of motion has no existence in external things. If they say: Motion is the body's being intermediate between the starting point and the endpoint, such that its state at any assumed instant within that duration is not similar to its state before or after, and this state is continuous and persistent. This indicates that the extension of motion is something existing in external things. We say: What exists in external things is the occurrence at this specific location, then at another specific location, and so on successively. This indicates that the extension of motion has no existence in external things. As for the demonstration that when this is the case, it is impossible for the quantity of that extension to have existence in external things, the proof for it is: The quantity of this extension is a property (ṣifa) of this extension, and if the qualified (al-mawṣūf) is impossible of existence in external things, then the property is even more deserving of being impossible of existence in external things. So it is established by what we have explained that the quantity of motion is impossible to exist in external things. But the Shaykh demonstrated by proofs that time (al-zamān) exists in external things. It follows that no part of time is the measure of motion.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the view that time is the measure of motion. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, it establishes that the extension of motion (imtidād al-ḥaraka) has no external existence because motion is a series of discrete occurrences at successive instants, and the sum of these occurrences does not exist simultaneously. Then it anticipates an objection: if motion is defined as a continuous state of being between two points, its extension might seem to persist. The answer is that what actually exists is only each discrete position at each instant, not the whole continuum. From the non-existence of the extension, it follows that its quantity (the measure) cannot exist either, because the quantity is a property of the extension, and if the subject does not exist, the property is even more impossible. Finally, since the Shaykh (presumably Avicenna or a later philosopher) has proven that time exists externally, it cannot be identical to the nonexistent quantity of motion. Thus, time is not the measure of motion.

الأعيان
مقدار الحركة
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because motion appears continuous in perception, its extension must exist externally as a single continuous entity.
Correction: The author argues that only discrete instants and positions exist externally; the continuity is only in our conception, not in reality.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can the quantity of the extension of motion not exist in external things?
A) Because motion does not exist at all.
B) Because the extension of motion has no existence in external things, so its property cannot exist.
C) Because time itself does not exist.
D) Because motion is a substance, not an accident.

Key Takeaway
The extension of motion does not exist externally, so time, which does exist, cannot be its measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author argues that a property of a non-existent thing is even more impossible to exist. Are there beliefs or attachments you hold about things whose reality you have never examined? Will you test them today?
?
If time exists externally but not as a measure of motion, then your actions are not tied to a continuous flow of time. How does this reshape your understanding of accountability for every single action in the discrete moment?

The Vicious Circle Argument against Time as Measure of Motion
Third argument: mutual dependence leads to vicious circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against time being the measure of motion, which demonstrates an impossible circular dependency.

Core Idea
If time depends on motion and motion depends on time, we have an impossible circle.

This lesson presents a concise logical argument that demolishes the definition of time as the measure of motion. By showing mutual dependency, the argument reveals a vicious circle (dawr bāṭil). We will follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the bracketed portions are editorial clarifications.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The argument begins: 'If time were the measure of motion...' Find this conditional and understand what follows from it.
3
Trace the dependency chain
Follow the reasoning: time would be a property of motion → properties depend on their subject → time depends on motion. But motion also depends on time (explanation given).
4
Recognize the conclusion: vicious circle
The mutual dependence leads to an impossible circle. Understand why this is considered a refutation of the definition.
The term 'al-dawr al-bāṭil al-muḥāl' means the false/impossible circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إبطال هذا المذهب : أن نقول : الزمان لو كان عبارة عن مقدار الحركة ، لكان الزمان صفة من صفات الحركة [ وكل صفة فهي محتاجة إلى الموصوف ] [ ينتج أن الزمان يجب أن يكون محتاجاً إلى الحركة ] لكن الحركة محتاجة إلى الزمان، لأن كل حركة فهي انتقال من حالة إلى حالة أخرى وزمان الحالة المنتقل عنها يجب أن يكون مغايراً لزمان الحالة المنتقل إليها ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، لم تتقرر ماهية الحركة إلا عند تقرر الزمان ، وتعاقب أجزائه . وحينئذ يلزم احتياج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر ، وذلك هو الدور الباطل المحال .
The third argument for refuting this doctrine: We say: If time were the measure of motion, then time would be a property (ṣifa) of motion [and every property is in need of its subject] [so it follows that time must be in need of motion]. But motion is in need of time, because every motion is a transition from one state to another, and the time of the state from which one is transferring must be different from the time of the state to which one is transferring. And when this is the case, the essence of motion is not established except with the establishment of time and the succession of its parts. Then it follows that each of them is in need of the other, and that is the false [or impossible] circle (al-dawr al-bāṭil al-muḥāl).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the view that time is the measure of motion. It is a classic circularity argument. The reasoning goes: if time is the measure of motion, then time is a property of motion. Every property depends on its subject, so time would depend on motion. But motion also depends on time, because motion involves transitions between states that require different times. Thus, motion's essence cannot be determined without time. Therefore, each depends on the other, creating a vicious circle (al-dawr al-bāṭil al-muḥāl), which is impossible. This refutes the definition.

Key Terms
الدور الباطل
مقدار الحركة
الزمان
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dependence is one-way (time depends on motion only, or motion depends on time only).
Correction: The argument shows that under the definition, both dependencies hold simultaneously, creating an absurd circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what logical problem arises if time is defined as the measure of motion?
A) Time would be prior to motion.
B) Each would be independent of the other.
C) Each would depend on the other, forming a vicious circle.
D) Motion would be impossible without time.

Key Takeaway
The third argument refutes the definition of time as the measure of motion by exposing a vicious circular dependency between time and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself—your identity, your worth, or your purpose—that might rest on circular reasoning. Are you willing to examine that foundation today and replace it with something sound?
?
This lesson shows that false starting assumptions lead to contradiction. What is one foundational assumption in your worldview that you have never critically examined? Will you test it against revealed truth?
?
The scholars rejected a definition because it led to a vicious circle. In your own life, are there habits or thoughts that create a cycle of self-justification but no real progress? What step can you take today to break that circle?

The Fourth Argument: Time Cannot Be the Measure of Motion
Presents the fourth proof that time cannot be the measure of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument refutes the definition of time as the measure of motion by showing it leads to the impossibility of a thing occurring in itself.

Core Idea
If time measures motion, then a day becomes a property of itself—an impossibility.

The fourth argument takes a concrete example—daily motion—and shows that defining time as the measure of motion leads to a logical contradiction. The author argues that we know daily motion occurs within a day with all its properties. If the day is just a property of that motion, then that property would have to occur in itself, which is rationally impossible. This argument strengthens the case that time cannot be merely a measure of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument's structure.
Notice the argument starts with a self-evident fact.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with a self-evident fact: daily motion occurs in a day with all its properties.
3
Understand the conditional
If the day were a property of motion, then that property would occur in itself.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Recognize the absurdity
A thing occurring in itself is impossible, so the original definition is false.
This is the conclusion.
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on why this argument is effective and how it fits into the broader discussion of time.
Consider the role of self-evident knowledge in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن كل عاقل يعلم بالبديهة أن الحركة اليومية ، سواء كانت حركة فلكية أو عنصرية، فإنها إنما حصلت بذاتها ، وبجميع صفاتها في هذا اليوم ، فلو كان هذا اليوم عبارة عن صفة من صفات بعض هذه الحركات ، لكان ذلك حكماً بأن تلك الصفة حصلت في نفس تلك الصفة ، وكان ذلك قولاً بحصول الشيء في نفسه ، وأنه باطل محال .
The fourth argument: Every rational person knows by self-evidence that the daily motion, whether celestial or elemental, has occurred in itself and with all its properties in this day (al-yawm). So if this day were a property of some of these motions, that would be a judgment that that property occurs in the very same property, and that would be saying that a thing occurs in itself, which is false and impossible.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which relies on self-evident knowledge. We know that the motion of a day (e.g., the sun's motion) takes place within that day, and the day itself contains the motion and its properties. If the day were defined as a property of that motion (i.e., its measure), then the day (a property) would be predicated of itself, because the motion's property would be said to occur within that same motion. This is the fallacy of a thing occurring in itself (ḥuṣūl al-shayʾ fī nafsihi), which is impossible. Therefore, the definition is false.

Key Terms

حصول الشيء في نفسه

Misconception: One might think that a day is just a period of time, not a property of motion.
Correction: The argument treats 'day' as a property under the definition being refuted; the absurdity arises from that definition.

Checkpoint

B) The day would be a property of itself.
C) Motion would be eternal.
D) Time would be a substance.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that defining time as the measure of motion leads to the impossible consequence of a thing occurring in itself.

The Fifth Argument against Time Being the Measure of Motion (Incomplete)
Argument from supposition: if time is the measure of motion, then no motion implies no time — but we perceive time without motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fifth argument against time being the measure of motion, including its conditional premise and the two proofs offered, even though the argument is incomplete in the text.

Core Idea
Time flows like water even when all motion is stilled — time is not dependent on motion.

In this passage, the author launches the fifth proof that time cannot be the measure of motion. He constructs a conditional argument: if time were the measure of motion, then when motion is absent, time should be impossible. But he argues that we can still perceive time passing even without motion. The text presents two supporting illustrations, though the second is cut off mid-sentence. This lesson unpacks the argument as far as it goes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then... but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false.'
The passage is incomplete; the second proof is cut off.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The argument begins with a conditional: if time were the measure of motion, then assuming no motion would make time impossible. Find this in the text.
3
Examine the first proof
The first proof asks us to imagine all motion stopped, including celestial and bodily motions. Yet we still find in the intellect something flowing like water. This is time itself, known necessarily after reflection.
4
Examine the second proof (incomplete)
The second proof considers the non-existence of the cosmos after its existence. This 'afterness' is temporal, implying time. The text cuts off here, but the intended conclusion is that time exists independently of motion.
5
Reflect on the argument's goal
The argument aims to show that time is not merely the measure of motion, because time can be conceived without motion. Think about why this matters for the nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كان الزمان مقداراً للحركة ، لكنا متى فرضنا عدم الحركة ، وجب أن يتعذر علينا فرض وجود الزمان، لكن التالي باطل ، فالمقدم مثله باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن فرض وجود مقدار الحركة ، مع أنه لا وجود للحركة : محال في العقول. كما أن فرض وجود مقدار الجسم ، مع أنه لا جسم : محال في العقول. وبيان بطلان التالي من وجهين : أحدهما : إنا لو فرضنا كون الفلك والشمس والقمر [ وسائر الكواكب واقفة ] ساكنة وبالغنا في تسكين كل الحركات حتى النفس والطرف [ وغيرهما ] ، فإنا نجد في صريح العقل وجود شيء يمر كالماء السيال ، والعلم به ضروري بعد الاعتبار . وثانيهما : إنا إذا فرضنا عدم الفلك والشمس والقمر ، وعدم حركاتها فلا بد وأن يقع عدمها بعد وجودها ، وهذه البعدية بالزمان ، فالذهن حال ما فرض
The fifth argument: If time were the measure of motion, then whenever we assume the absence of motion, it would be impossible for us to assume the existence of time. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: The assumption of the existence of the measure of motion while there is no motion is intellectually impossible, just as the assumption of the existence of the measure of a body while there is no body is intellectually impossible. And the demonstration of the falsity of the consequent is from two aspects: One: If we assume the sphere, the sun, the moon, and all the planets to be stationary, and we go to the extreme in stilling all motions, even the breath and the eyelid [and others], then we find in clear intellect the existence of something that flows like flowing water, and knowledge of it is necessary after reflection. The second: If we assume the non-existence of the sphere, the sun, and the moon, and the non-existence of their motions, then their non-existence must occur after their existence, and this afterness is temporal (bi-l-zamān), so the mind when it assumes...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth argument, which is an argument from supposition. The argument is a conditional: if time were the measure of motion, then assuming the absence of motion would entail the impossibility of assuming time's existence (since a measure requires a measured subject). But the consequent is false, as demonstrated by two considerations. First, even if all motion (celestial and elemental, including breath and eyelid movement) is stilled, we still perceive something passing like flowing water — this is time itself, known necessarily after reflection. Second, if we assume the annihilation of all moving things, their non-existence occurs after their existence; this 'afterness' is temporal and presupposes time. The text cuts off here, but the argument likely concludes that time exists independently of motion, so it cannot be the measure of motion. Because the argument is incomplete, the full conclusion is not presented.
