The Seventh Proof: Time Equally Contains Motion and Rest
Al-Razi uses intuitive judgments to show time is not specific to motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses intuitive judgments and three decisive arguments to prove that time is an independent container for both motion and rest, not merely a measure of motion.

Core Idea
Time is a continuous container for both motion and rest, not just a measure of motion.

This lesson examines the seventh proof from al-Razi's arguments in al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The passage begins with an appeal to common intuition: we naturally apply temporal measurements to both motion and rest. After presenting this core insight, al-Razi anticipates an objection that rest is only hypothetically measured by time. He then delivers three decisive refutations, building a strong case that time is ontologically prior and independent of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the overall argument sink in before analyzing details.
Notice that the passage flows from intuition to objection to multiple rebuttals.
2
Grasp the initial intuitive claim
Identify the first sentence where al-Razi states that the intellect judges equally about motion and rest in relation to time. Why is this observation powerful?
It appeals to a universal, pre-theoretical awareness.
3
Understand the objection
Find the objection that rest is only measured by time hypothetically, by imagining motion instead. What does this objection try to preserve?
The opponent wants to save the definition of time as measure of motion.
4
Examine the three arguments
Read each of the three arguments al-Razi gives against the objection. For each, note the key idea: (1) continuous duration independent of motion, (2) circularity, (3) parallel intellectual judgments.
These arguments are cumulative – each alone suffices to refute the objection.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how al-Razi concludes that claiming time is derivative from motion is an arbitrary judgment. Ask yourself why this conclusion matters for the broader debate about time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن بديهة العقل كما حكمت بصحة أن يقال : الجسم تحرك من هذه الساعة الفلانية ، فكذلك حكمت بصحة أن يقال : الجسم سكن من هذه الساعة إلى الساعة الفلانية ، وذلك يقتضي الجزم بأن نسبة الزمان إلى الحركة وإلى السكون على السوية ، وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يقال : الزمان مقدار الحركة. فإن قالوا : السكون إنما يتقدر بالزمان على سبيل الفرض ، بمعنى أن الشيء الذي هو الآن ساكن ، لو فرضنا أنه كان متحركاً ، بدلاً عن كونه ساكناً ، لكانت تلك الحركة واقعة في هذا القدر من الزمان. فنقول : إن هذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا ذكرنا أن الشخص الغافل عن جميع الحركات التي في العالم الأعلى ، والعالم الأسفل يجد المدة والزمان أمراً مستمراً باقياً . وذلك يدل على أنه سواء كان الحاصل هو الحركة والسكون فإن هذه المدة تكون باقية . الثاني : إن ماهية الحركة مفتقرة في تحققها إلى حصول الزمان ، فلو كان الزمان مفتقراً في تحققه إلى حصول الحركة لزم الدور . والثالث : إن العقل كما حكم بأن الحركة لا يمكن وقوعها ، إلا في زمان مخصوص ، فكذلك حكمه بكون الزمان ظرفاً للسكون ، لا يتوقف على استحضار معنى الحركة ، بل حكم العقل بكون الزمان ظرفاً للحركة تارة ، وللسكون أخرى على السوية ، ولا نجد في العقل بين البابين تفاوتاً أصلا . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان القول بأن كون الزمان مقداراً للسكون ، تابعاً لكونه مقداراً للحركة حكماً محضاً.
The seventh proof: The innate intuition of the intellect, just as it judged the correctness of saying 'the body moved from such-and-such an hour', likewise it judged the correctness of saying 'the body rested from this hour to that hour', and that necessitates certainty that the relation of time to motion and to rest is equal. And if that is so, it is impossible to say that time is the measure of motion. If they say: 'Rest is only measured by time in a hypothetical manner, meaning that the thing which is now at rest, if we suppose it had moved instead of being at rest, that motion would occur in this amount of time.' We say: This is false. Evidence for it are [the following] points: First: We mentioned that a person who is unaware of all motions in the upper world and the lower world finds that duration and time are a continuous persisting thing. That indicates that whether what is occurring is motion or rest, this duration remains. Second: The essence of motion is dependent for its realization on the occurrence of time; if time were dependent for its realization on the occurrence of motion, a vicious circle would follow. Third: The intellect, just as it judged that motion cannot occur except in a specific time, likewise its judgment that time is a container for rest does not depend on bringing to mind the meaning of motion; rather the intellect judges that time is a container for motion sometimes and for rest at other times equally, and we do not find in the intellect any difference between the two cases at all. And if that is so, then the claim that time's being a measure for rest is derivative from its being a measure for motion is a purely arbitrary judgment.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which refutes the idea that time is a measure of motion. It begins by appealing to intuitive judgment: just as we say motion occurred from one hour to another, we also say rest occurred from one hour to another. This shows that time relates equally to motion and rest, so it cannot be essentially tied to motion. The proof then anticipates an objection: perhaps rest is measured by time only hypothetically, by supposing the object were moving instead. The author refutes this with three arguments. First, a person unaware of any motion still perceives duration as continuous, proving time exists independently. Second, motion depends on time; if time depended on motion, there would be circularity. Third, the intellect's judgment that time contains rest does not require imagining motion; both judgments are parallel. Thus, the claim that time is primarily for motion is arbitrary. The passage is a careful refutation of a key view in Aristotelian physics, defending time's ontological primacy.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل (innate intuition of intellect)
الحركة (motion)
السكون (rest)
نسبة الزمان (relation of time)
مقدار الحركة (measure of motion)
الدور (vicious circle)
الفرض (hypothesis)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is only definable through motion; rest is measured by time only hypothetically.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that time is a fundamental container that equally holds both motion and rest, and its relation to rest is not derived from motion.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the seventh proof?
A) Time is a measure of motion only.
B) Time relates equally to motion and rest.
C) Rest is measured by time only hypothetically.
D) Motion depends on time and vice versa.

Key Takeaway
Time is an independent continuum that serves as a container for both motion and rest equally, and the idea that time is only a measure of motion is rationally indefensible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You often measure your life by what you accomplish—motion, activity, productivity. But what if your periods of rest are just as real and significant in the container of time? How does this truth change the way you view the 'empty' or 'still' moments of your day? Will you begin to see them as equally meaningful?

?
You might have thought that time only moves when you are doing something. But this lesson shows that even when you are still, time continues. What are you doing with your rest? Are you truly resting or just wasting time? How can you make your stillness intentional?

Three Proofs That Time Is Independent of Motion
Three proofs refuting the dependence of time on motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs demonstrating that time is not intrinsically dependent on motion but is an independent container for both motion and rest.

Core Idea
Time is not a product of motion; it is an independent container for both motion and rest.

In this passage, the author presents three compelling arguments against the view that time is essentially tied to motion. He first appeals to our intuitive perception of duration independent of motion, then uses a logical argument based on circularity, and finally invokes reason's equal judgment of time as a container for motion and rest. These proofs establish time as an independent entity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target view
Read the opening sentence to understand the view being refuted: that if a stationary object had been moving, that motion would occur in the same duration. This claim treats time as essentially tied to motion.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof argues that a person unaware of all motions still perceives time as a continuous duration, showing time exists regardless of motion or rest.
This relies on direct human experience.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof uses the logical problem of circularity: motion depends on time for its existence, so if time also depended on motion, a vicious circle would result.
This is a self-consistency argument.
4
Examine the third proof
The third proof points out that reason judges time to be a container for rest just as much as for motion, without needing to recall motion. Thus, time is not solely a measure of motion.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the three proofs to see that time is an independent dimension, not dependent on motion. The claim that time is only a measure of motion is thus arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبيل الفرض ، بمعنى أن الشيء الذي هو الآن ساكن ، لو فرضنا أنه كان متحركاً ، بدلاً عن كونه ساكناً ، لكانت تلك الحركة واقعة في هذا القدر من الزمان. فنقول : إن هذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إنا ذكرنا أن الشخص الغافل عن جميع الحركات التي في العالم الأعلى ، [ والعالم ] الأسفل يجد المدة [ والزمان أمراً مستمراً باقياً . وذلك يدل على أنه سواء كان الحاصل هو الحركة والسكون فإن هذه المدة ] تكون باقية
الثاني : إن ماهية الحركة مفتقرة في تحققها إلى حصول الزمان ، فلو كان الزمان مفتقراً في تحققه إلى حصول الزمان لزم الدور .
والثالث : إن العقل كما حكم بأن الحركة لا يمكن وقوعها ، إلا في زمان مخصوص ، فكذلك حكمه بكون الزمان ظرفاً للسكون ، لا يتوقف على استحضار معنى الحركة ، بل حكم العقل بكون الزمان ظرفاً للحركة تارة ، وللسكون أخرى على السوية ، ولا نجد في العقل بين البابين تفاوتاً أصلا . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، كان القول بأن كون الزمان مقداراً للسكون ، تابعاً لكونه مقداراً للحركة [ حكماً محضاً ]
The way of supposition: that is, if we suppose that a thing which is now at rest had been moving, instead of being at rest, that motion would have occurred in this amount of time. So we say: this is false. And proofs for it are several:
First: We have mentioned that a person unaware of all motions in the higher and lower worlds perceives duration [and time] as a continuous, enduring thing. That indicates that whether the result is motion or rest, this duration remains.
Second: The essence of motion depends in its actuality on the occurrence of time. So if time depended in its actuality on the occurrence of motion, circularity would result.
Third: Reason, just as it judges that motion cannot occur except in a specific time, likewise judges that time is a container for rest without needing to recall the meaning of motion. Rather, reason judges that time is a container for motion sometimes and for rest at other times equally, and we find no difference in reason between the two cases. If this is so, then saying that time is a measure of rest dependent on its being a measure of motion is a pure judgment.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs against the view that time is essentially tied to motion. The author states the view to be refuted: that if a stationary object had been moving, that motion would have occurred in the same duration. He declares this false and gives three proofs. The first proof: a person unaware of all motions still perceives time as a continuous duration, showing time exists regardless of motion or rest. The second proof: the essence of motion requires time for its existence, so if time required motion, there would be circularity. The third proof: reason judges that time is equally a container for motion and rest, so it is not solely a measure of motion. Thus, the claim that time is only a measure of motion is arbitrary.

Key Terms
زمان
حركة
سكون
مقدار
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is merely a property or measure of motion.
Correction: The proofs show that time is independent: it exists even without motion (perception), it cannot depend on motion without circularity, and reason treats it as equally containing rest.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof argue?
A) Time is independent of motion.
B) Motion requires time, so if time required motion there would be circularity.
C) A person unaware of motion still perceives time.
D) Reason judges time equally for motion and rest.

Key Takeaway
Time is an independent dimension that contains both motion and rest, as proven by perception, logical necessity, and rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you measure your life's value by your productivity and motion, forgetting that time itself is a gift from Allah that contains both your busy moments and your stillness? How can you begin to see time as a divine container rather than a resource to exploit?
?
If time does not depend on motion, then your rest is as real and meaningful as your action. In what ways have you felt guilty for stillness, and how does this truth free you to honor both?
?
The passage shows that reason judges time as a container for rest equally. Yet you may treat rest as secondary. What would it look like to intentionally rest as an act of worship, knowing that Allah created time to hold your silence as much as your movement?

The Eighth Proof: Motion's Extent Has No Existence
Argument that extent of motion has no existence in past, present, or future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the extent of motion cannot exist in any time and how this argument undermines the reality of time as a measure of motion.

Core Idea
The extent of motion cannot be found in the past, present, or future—so it must be unreal.

This passage presents a powerful proof from the kalām tradition that the extent of motion—the duration or measure of a movement—has no actual existence in the external world. The argument proceeds by examining all possible temporal locations where such an extent could be found and shows each to be impossible. It is a classic demonstration of how logical reasoning can eliminate seemingly plausible metaphysical concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the three possibilities considered.
The proof is structured as a dilemma: if the extent exists, it must be in one of three times – but all are impossible.
2
Understand the impossibility of the present
The present moment is indivisible, but the extent of motion is divisible. A divisible thing cannot exist in an indivisible container. Why is that a logical contradiction?
This parallels the atomist idea that space and time are composed of indivisible parts.
3
Analyze the two arguments against past and future
First argument: past and future are non-existent. Second argument: if past and future are composed of instants, time would be a series of indivisible nows, which the philosophers reject. Grasp both lines.
The second argument is more subtle and targets the notion of time as continuous flow.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since all three temporal locations are impossible, the extent of motion has no external existence. Reflect on what this means for time itself.
The proof is not just about motion but challenges the reality of temporal extension.
5
Test your understanding with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can articulate the reason for the present's impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : لو حصل لمقدار امتداد الحركة وجود ، لكان ذلك المقدار إما أن يكون حاصلاً في الحال، أو في الماضي أو في المستقبل، والكل باطل. أما أن يكون حاضراً في الحال، فلان الحال الحاضر لا يقبل الانقسام، [أنه يمنع؟] ومقدار امتداد الحركة أمر منقسم ، وحصول المنقسم في غير المنقسم محال
وأما الثاني والثالث فهما أيضاً باطلان لوجهين :
الأول : إن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان ، ولا شيء من المعدوم بموجود
[ينتج] : فلا شيء من الماضي والمستقبل بموجود
الثاني : وهو أن الماضي هو الذي كان حاضراً ثم انقضى ، والمستقبل هو الذي يتوقع حضوره إلا أنه بعد لم يحضر ، لكن الحاضر ليس إلا الآن ، الذي لا يقبل القسمة ، فالماضي والمستقبل على هذا التقدير ليس إلا الآنات الحاضرة ، التي صارت ماضية، وذلك يوجب كون الزمان مركباً من الأنات المتتالية ، وهو عند القوم محال . فثبت : أن مقدار الحركة لو كان موجوداً ، لكان وجوده إما أن يحصل في الحال، أو في الماضي، أو في المستقبل . وثبت أن كل هذه الأقسام باطلة ، فوجب الجزم بأن مقدار امتداد الحركة لا وجود له في الأعيان البتة .
The eighth proof: If the extent of motion had existence, then that extent would either be present in the present moment, or in the past, or in the future, all of which are false. As for its being present in the present moment, because the present moment does not admit division, [it is impossible?] and the extent of motion is divisible, and the occurrence of the divisible in the indivisible is impossible.
As for the second and third, they are also false for two reasons:
First: The past and future are non-existent, and nothing non-existent is existent. [So it follows]: Nothing of the past and future is existent.
Second: The past is what was present and then elapsed, and the future is what is expected to become present but has not yet become present. However, the present is only the instant, which does not admit division. So the past and future on this estimation are only present instants that have become past, and that necessitates that time be composed of successive instants, which according to the people [the philosophers] is impossible. So it is established: if the extent of motion were existent, its existence would either be in the present, or the past, or the future. And it is established that all these divisions are false. So it is necessary to conclude that the extent of motion has no existence in external things at all.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is the eighth proof against the reality of time as an attribute of motion. It argues that if the extent of motion had existence, it would have to be located either in the present, the past, or the future. Each possibility is shown to be impossible. The present is indivisible, but the extent of motion is divisible, so it cannot be in the present. The past and future are non-existent, so they cannot contain anything existent. Furthermore, if past and future are reduced to a series of present instants, time would be composed of discrete instants, which the philosophers reject. Therefore, the extent of motion has no objective existence at all. This argument undermines the idea that time is a real measure of motion.

Key Terms
مقدار امتداد
الحال
الماضي
المستقبل
الآن
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion's extent is an actual, objectively existing feature of the world, like a continuous duration.
Correction: The proof shows that if it existed, it would have to be located in a time, but no time can contain it, so it is a logical impossibility. Hence, extent of motion is not real in the external world.

Checkpoint

A) The present is only a single instant.
B) The present is divisible.
C) The present is non-existent.
D) The extent of motion is indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The extent of motion has no real existence in the external world because it cannot be located in any moment of time—past, present, or future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience time as a continuous flow of moments. But this proof claims that motion's extent has no real existence. How does this challenge your everyday experience of time and movement? Will you now question what you once took for granted about the passing of time?
?
If the extent of motion is not real, then what is the nature of your actions? Are they also momentary and unreal? This lesson forces you to reconsider the reality of your own movements. Can you accept that your actions are merely successive instants with no underlying duration?
?
The proof relies on the impossibility of divisible things in indivisible moments. You live your life in moments that seem to stretch. But are you actually living in a series of indivisible 'nows'? How does this affect your understanding of your own existence and the passage from one moment to the next?

Ninth Proof: Body in Time and Critique of Ibn Sina's Response
Argument that bodies exist in time similar to motions, and refutation of Ibn Sina's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that time cannot be exclusively a measure of motion, and why Ibn Sina's response is considered weak.

Core Idea
Bodies and motions are equally in time – time's relation to both is identical.

This lesson presents the ninth proof in the series, which argues that reason judges bodies to be in time just as it judges motions to be in time. It then presents Ibn Sina's response and the author's sharp critique, showing that the Sheikh's answer fails to address the real question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Note the three parts: proof, response, and critique.
2
Identify the core argument
Notice that the author draws an equivalence between body and motion regarding time, then uses this to challenge the definition of time as measure of motion.
3
Understand the critique
See how the author shows that Ibn Sina's explanation only pushes the problem back, without explaining what it means for a body to be in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إنا كما نحكم بأن هذه الحركة حصلت في هذا الزمان ، فكذلك نحكم بأن هذا الجسم ، حصل في هذا الزمان ، ولا نجد في العقل تفاوتاً بين قولنا : حصلت هذه الحركة في هذا الزمان ، وبين قولنا : حصل هذا الجسم في هذا الزمان، وإذا كان كذلك ، كانت نسبة وجود الزمان إلى الحركة ، كنسبته إلى الجسم ، وذلك يمنع من كون الزمان مقداراً للحركة
أجاب الشيخ عنه . وقال : إنما يقال : و الجسم في الزمان ، بمعنى أنه في الحركة [ والحركة ] في الزمان ، وهذا هو الذي نص عليه في كتاب عيون الحكمة . وذكره أيضاً في كتاب الشفاء .
واعلم أنه ضعيف جداً ، وذلك لأن الزمان لما كان مقداراً للحركة ، كان عرضاً موجوداً في الحركة ، والحركة عرض موجود في الجسم ، وذلك يقتضي كون الزمان موجوداً في الجسم، فبهذا الطريق يظهر كون الزمان موجوداً في الجسم ، ولا يظهر منه معنى كون الجسم موجوداً في الزمان . والبحث إنما وقع عن معنى قولنا : الجسم موجود في الزمان . فأين أحد الكلامين من الآخر .
The ninth proof: Just as we judge that this motion occurred in this time, so we judge that this body occurred in this time, and we find no difference in reason between our saying 'this motion occurred in this time' and our saying 'this body occurred in this time'. If this is so, then the relation of time's existence to motion is like its relation to body, and that prevents time from being a measure of motion.
The Sheikh answered concerning it and said: It is only said that the body is in time, meaning that it is in motion and motion is in time. This is what he stated in the book 'Uyun al-Hikma and also mentioned in al-Shifa'.
Know that this is very weak, because since time is a measure of motion, it is an accident existing in motion, and motion is an accident existing in the body. That necessitates that time exists in the body. By this way, it appears that time exists in the body, but the meaning of 'the body exists in time' does not appear from it. The inquiry is only about the meaning of our saying 'the body exists in time'. So where is one of the two statements from the other?
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is the ninth proof and its response. The proof argues that reason judges that bodies exist in time just as motions do, with no difference. Therefore, time relates equally to motion and body, contradicting the idea that time is only a measure of motion. The Sheikh (Ibn Sina) replies that saying 'body in time' means 'body in motion and motion in time'. The author criticizes this as very weak: if time is an accident in motion, and motion an accident in body, then time exists in the body, not the body in time. The question is about the meaning of 'body exists in time', which remains unexplained. Thus, the Sheikh's answer does not address the issue.

حركة

عرض
شيخ

Misconception: One might think Ibn Sina's explanation satisfactorily accounts for how bodies are in time.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the Sheikh's response does not explain the meaning of 'body exists in time'; it merely asserts a different relation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author argues that bodies are directly in time, not merely through motion, and that Ibn Sina's response fails to address this point.

The Ninth Argument: Does Time Measure Body or Motion?
Al-Razi presents the ninth argument and critiques Ibn Sina's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that time cannot be exclusively a measure of motion, and why he critiques Ibn Sina's response as missing the point.

Core Idea
Time relates to body and motion equally; Ibn Sina's reply fails to address the real question.

Al-Razi presents a challenging argument against the standard Aristotelian definition of time as the measure of motion. He observes that we say 'the body is in time' just as we say 'motion is in time,' so time cannot be a measure unique to motion. Ibn Sina attempts to salvage the definition by claiming that body is in time only indirectly through motion. But al-Razi finds this response weak and exposes its logical flaw. Let's examine this argument, the reply, and the critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the three parts: al-Razi's argument, Ibn Sina's reply, and al-Razi's critique.
Focus on the logical parallelism al-Razi highlights.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify what al-Razi argues (time relates to body equally) and what Ibn Sina replies (body is in time only via motion).
Notice how each uses the phrase 'in time' differently.
3
Understand the critique
Al-Razi says Ibn Sina's response shows time exists in the body (as an accident) but does not explain the body's existence in time. Understand why this is a different question.
The key is distinguishing between 'time in body' and 'body in time'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إنا كما نحكم بأن هذه الحركة حصلت في هذا الزمان ، فكذلك نحكم بأن هذا الجسم ، حصل في هذا الزمان ، ولا نجد في العقل تفاوتاً بين قولنا : حصلت هذه الحركة في هذا الزمان ، وبين قولنا : حصل هذا الجسم في هذا الزمان، وإذا كان كذلك ، كانت نسبة وجود الزمان إلى الحركة ، كنسبته إلى الجسم ، وذلك يمنع من كون الزمان مقداراً للحركة . أجاب الشيخ عنه . وقال : إنما يقال : و الجسم في الزمان ، بمعنى أنه في الحركة والحركة في الزمان ، وهذا هو الذي نص عليه في كتاب عيون الحكمة . وذكره أيضاً في كتاب الشفاء . واعلم أنه ضعيف جداً ، وذلك لأن الزمان لما كان مقداراً للحركة ، كان عرضاً موجوداً في الحركة ، والحركة عرض موجود في الجسم ، وذلك يقتضي كون الزمان موجوداً في الجسم، فبهذا الطريق يظهر كون الزمان موجوداً في الجسم ، ولا يظهر منه معنى كون الجسم موجوداً في الزمان . والبحث إنما وقع عن معنى قولنا : الجسم موجود في الزمان . فأين أحد الكلامين من الآخر ؟
The ninth proof: Just as we judge that this motion occurred in this time, so we judge that this body occurred in this time, and we find no difference in the mind between our saying 'this motion occurred in this time' and 'this body occurred in this time.' If that is the case, then the relation of the existence of time to motion is like its relation to the body, and that prevents time from being a measure of motion. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) replied to him and said: 'The body is said to be in time meaning that it is in motion, and motion is in time.' This is what he stated in the book 'Uyun al-Hikma and also mentioned in al-Shifa'. But know that it is very weak. That is because when time is the measure of motion, it is an accident existing in motion, and motion is an accident existing in the body. That necessitates that time exists in the body. So in this way, it appears that time exists in the body, but it does not show the meaning of the body existing in time. The inquiry is about the meaning of our saying 'the body exists in time.' So where is one of these two statements from the other?
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument and its refutation. The author argues that we predicate time of both motion and body equally, so time cannot be a measure specific to motion. Ibn Sina responds by saying that the body is in time indirectly through motion. The author then criticizes this as very weak, because if time is an accident of motion, and motion is an accident of the body, then time exists in the body as an accident of an accident, not that the body exists in time. The question is about the meaning of the body's existence in time, and Ibn Sina's answer does not address it.

Correction: Al-Razi uses the parallelism to argue against the definition, but Ibn Sina's attempt to reduce one to the other fails because the two statements have different logical implications.

The Second Argument for Time and Its Refutation
Opponents' argument and author's refutation on time as quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' argument that time is a quantity and the measure of motion, and the author's refutation that time's receptivity to equality does not make it a quantity in itself.

Core Idea
Time's receptivity to equality does not make it a quantity in itself.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated argument that time must be a quantity and ultimately the measure of motion, based on its receptivity to equality and difference. The author refutes this by distinguishing between essential and relational receptivity, using the remarkable example of God's eternal duration to show that even such receptivity does not entail quantity or physical change.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument and refutation.
Notice the structure: a premise, then the opponents' argument, then the refutation.
2
Trace the opponents' argument chain
Identify each step: time is receptive to equality/difference → time is a quantity → non-permanent quantity → requires matter → matter is motion → time is measure of motion.
Each step is defended with an objection against alternatives.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author denies the key premise that receptivity implies quantity in itself. He argues time may receive equality through relations, not essence. The example of God's duration shows that even something eternal can be longer or shorter without being a quantity or subject to change.
This refutation targets the core assumption that time's property is intrinsic.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this lesson clarifies the importance of essence-accident distinction in kalam arguments about time.
The opponents' argument fails because it mistakes a relational property for an essential one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا قد ذكرنا أن الدلائل القاهرة على أن القبلية والمعية والبعدية لا وجود لها في الأعيان، وأنها مجرد نسب يعتبرها الذهن، ويتصورها العقل. الحجة الثانية لهم: قالوا إنا قد بينا في دليل الإمكانات: أن الزمان قابل للمساواة والمفاوتة. ثم نقول: وكل ما كان كذلك فهو كم. ينتج: فالزمان كم. ثم نقول: وكل كم إما أن يكون قار الذات أو لا يكون، والأول باطل، وإلا كان الوقت الحاضر عين الماضي وعين المستقبل. هذا خلف. فثبت: أنه كم غير قار الذات. وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون أجزاؤه على التقضي والمرور. وكل ما كان كذلك فله مادة، يكون هو صفة من صفاتها، وحالة من أحوالها بناء على أنه لا بد لكل محدث من مادة. فالزمان لا بد له من مادة. ومادته إما مادة الجسم، وإما أن تكون مادته صفة من صفات الجسم. والأول باطل، لأن الزمان إن جعل مقداراً لمادة المسافة فهو محال، لأن المختلفين في هذا المقدار قد يستويان في المسافة. وإن جعل مقداراً لمادة المتحرك فهو أيضاً محال، وإلا لكان كلما كان الزمان أعظم، كان المتحرك أعظم. هذا خلف. فثبت: أن الزمان مقدار لهيئة حالة في الجسم، وتلك الهيئة إما قارة وإما غير قارة. والأول باطل لأن مقدار الهيئة القارة يجب أن يكون قاراً، وهذا المقدار غير قار. فبقي أن الزمان مقدار لهيئة غير قارة، وهي الحركة. فثبت: أن الزمان مقدار الحركة. والجواب: لا نسلم أن كل ما كان قابلاً للمساواة والمفاوتة كان كما بالذات. بل كل ما قبل المساواة والمفاوتة لذاته، لا لأجل غيره، كان كماً بالذات. وأنتم ما أقمتم الدلالة على أن الزمان قابل للمساواة والمفاوتة لذاته. فإنه ليس يمتنع أن يقال: الزمان له في ذاته حقيقة مستقلة بنفسها قائمة بذاتها، ثم تعرض لها نسب وإضافات إلى الحركات، فيعرض لها بسبب تلك النسب والإضافات قبولها للمساواة والمفاوتة. وهذا القدر لا يوجب كون الزمان كما بالذات، ولا يقضي عليه بكونه [؟] في ذاته، منقضياً بحسب أجزاء ماهيته. والذي يحقق هذا الكلام تجسّم مادة هذا الخيال. فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن دوام ذات الله تعالى ألف سنة، أطول من دوام ذاته مائة سنة، ومن أنكر ذلك لم يلتفت إليه. فثبت: أن دوام ذاته قابل للمساواة واللامساواة. لا يلزم منه كون ذاته جسماً متحركاً. فكذا ههنا.
The third: We have mentioned that the decisive proofs show that priority, simultaneity, and posteriority have no existence in external entities, and that they are mere relations considered by the intellect and conceived by the mind. Their second argument: They said: We have established in the proof of possibilities that time is receptive to equality and difference. Then we say: Everything that is thus is a quantity. The conclusion: time is a quantity. Then we say: Every quantity is either permanent in substance or not. The first is false, otherwise the present time would be identical to the past and identical to the future. This is absurd. So it is established that it is a non-permanent-in-substance quantity. And if it is thus, then its parts must be subject to passing and elapsing. And everything that is thus has a matter, being a quality of its attributes and a state of its states, based on the fact that every originated thing must have a matter. So time must have a matter. Its matter is either the matter of a body or its matter is an attribute of a body. The first is false, because if time is made a measure of the matter of distance, it is impossible, since two differing in this measure can be equal in distance. And if it is made a measure of the matter of the moving object, it is also impossible, otherwise whenever time is greater, the moving object would be greater. This is absurd. So it is established that time is a measure of a state in a body, and that state is either permanent or non-permanent. The first is false, because the measure of a permanent state must be permanent, and this measure is non-permanent. So it remains that time is a measure of a non-permanent state, which is motion. So it is established that time is the measure of motion. The answer: We do not grant that everything receptive to equality and difference is a quantity in itself. Rather, everything that receives equality and difference in itself, not due to another, is a quantity in itself. You have not demonstrated that time is receptive to equality and difference in itself. For it is not impossible to say: time in itself has an independent reality subsisting in itself, then relations and additions to motions occur to it, and due to those relations and additions it becomes receptive to equality and difference. This does not necessitate time being a quantity in itself, nor does it require it to be [unclear] in itself, elapsing in the parts of its essence. And what confirms this statement is the embodiment of the material of this imagination. For we know by necessity that the duration of the essence of God, may He be exalted, for one thousand years is longer than the duration of His essence for one hundred years. Whoever denies that is not paid attention to. So it is established that the duration of His essence is receptive to equality and inequality, yet it does not necessitate His essence being a moving body. The same applies here.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents a premise and the opponents' second argument about the nature of time, followed by the author's refutation. The author begins by asserting that priority, simultaneity, and posteriority have no external existence; they are mere mental relations. This sets the stage for the opponents' second argument. The opponents argue that time is a quantity because it is receptive to equality and difference. They reason: every such thing is a quantity; therefore time is a quantity. They further divide quantity into permanent and non-permanent; time cannot be permanent because then past, present, and future would coincide. So time is a non-permanent quantity, implying its parts pass away. Such a thing requires a matter of which it is an attribute. The opponents consider two possibilities for this matter: the matter of a body or an attribute of a body. They reject the first because time as a measure of distance could be equal for different distances, and as a measure of the moving object it would make the object's size vary with time. So they conclude time must be a measure of a state in a body, and that state must be non-permanent, i.e., motion. Hence time is the measure of motion. The author refutes by denying that receptivity to equality and difference inherently makes something a quantity. He insists that only if it is receptive in itself, not due to relations, does it become a quantity. The opponents have not proven that time is receptive in itself. Time may have its own essence and then receive equality and difference through relations to motions. This does not make it a quantity or entail that it is essentially passing. As an example, the duration of God's essence is longer in one thousand years than in one hundred years, yet God is not a moving body. Similarly, time's receptivity does not require it to be a quantity or a measure of motion.

قار الذات
غير قار الذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument is valid because time obviously seems to be measured, so it must be a quantity.
Correction: The author shows that time's receptivity to equality and difference may be due to relations to motions, not its own essence, so it does not prove time is a quantity in itself.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise the opponents rely on in their second argument?
A) Every quantity is either permanent or non-permanent.
B) Everything receptive to equality and difference is a quantity.
C) Time has a matter.
D) Time is the measure of motion.

Key Takeaway
Time's apparent receptivity to equality does not force it to be a quantity in itself; it may arise from relations to motion, similar to how God's eternal duration admits longer and shorter without implying quantity or change.

?

?
The author uses God's eternal duration as proof that length of existence does not imply being a quantity or changing. When you think about your own existence, do you measure your worth by the amount of time you have? How does this lesson free you from that mindset?
?
You often say 'I don't have enough time.' But according to this lesson, time is not a substance you possess. What would it mean to stop treating time as a resource and start treating it as a framework for your relationship with Allah?

Third Argument for Time's Dependence on Motion and Its Refutation
Presents the third argument that time awareness depends on motion awareness and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that time awareness depends on motion awareness and how the author refutes it using the counterexample of a blind person in a dark room.

Core Idea
Time awareness can exist without motion awareness.

This passage presents a third argument from those who claim time is an accident of motion, based on a correlation between awareness of motion and awareness of time. They provide examples from everyday experience and the story of the Companions of the Cave. The author refutes by first clarifying that sleep blocks all awareness, not just motion awareness, and then offers a powerful counterexample: a blind person in a dark room who perceives no motions yet is conscious of the passage of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument and Examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the examples given: the distressed person who feels time drags, the joyful person who feels time flies, and the sleepers in the Cave.
2
Identify the Reasoning
The opponents argue that because time awareness correlates with motion awareness, time must be an accident of motion. Understand this causal assumption.
Correlation does not necessarily imply causation.
3
Read the Refutation
The author gives two refutation points: first, sleep prevents all consciousness, not just motion consciousness; second, a blind person in a dark room perceives no motion yet is aware of time.
The counterexample directly challenges the correlation-causation leap.
4
Reflect on the Implications
Consider what this means for the nature of time. If time awareness can exist without motion awareness, then time is not essentially dependent on motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم: قالوا: الدليل على أن الزمان من لواحق الحركة: أنه كلما كان الشعور بالحركة أتم، كان الشعور بالزمان أتم. ولهذا السبب فإن المغتم بمرور الزمان قد يستطيله، والمستغرق بالطرب قد يستقصره. لأجل أن الأول شاعر بالحركات، والثاني غافل عنها. وأما في حق النائم فهو مثل ما يذكر في قصة أصحاب الكهف، فإنه لما لم يكن لهم شعور بالحركة، لا جرم لم يكن لهم شعور بالزمان. فالجواب: إن النائم إنما لم يشعر بالزمان، لأن النوم مانع من الشعور مطلقاً، لا لما قالوه من أن عدم شعوره بالحركة، اقتضى عدم شعوره بالزمان. ثم نقول: هذا بالعكس أولى، فإنا بينا: أن الأعمى الجالس في البيت المظلم الذي لا يحس بشيء من الحركات، فإنه يكون شاعراً بمرور المدة والزمان.
Their third argument: They said: The proof that time is among the accidents of motion is that whenever consciousness of motion is more complete, consciousness of time is more complete. For this reason, the one distressed by the passage of time may find it long, and the one absorbed in pleasure may find it short, because the first is conscious of motions and the second is heedless of them. As for the sleeper, it is like what is mentioned in the story of the Companions of the Cave: since they had no consciousness of motion, they had no consciousness of time. The answer: The sleeper lacks consciousness of time only because sleep prevents consciousness altogether, not because lack of motion consciousness necessitates lack of time consciousness. Then we say: This is actually the opposite, for we have shown that a blind person sitting in a dark room who perceives no motions is nonetheless conscious of the passage of duration and time.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the opponents' third argument and the author's refutation. The opponents argue that time is an accident of motion because awareness of time correlates with awareness of motion. They give examples: a person in distress feels time is long because they are conscious of motions, while someone absorbed in joy feels time is short because they are heedless of motions. The sleepers in the Cave, unaware of motion, were unaware of time. The author refutes by first noting that the sleeper's lack of time consciousness is due to sleep itself blocking all awareness, not specifically due to lack of motion awareness. He then provides a counterexample: a blind person in a dark room who perceives no motions is still conscious of the passage of time. This shows that time awareness can exist without motion awareness, contradicting the opponents' claim.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time awareness requires motion awareness.
Correction: The author shows that a blind person in a dark room, who perceives no motions, is still conscious of the passage of time, proving that time awareness can exist independently of motion awareness.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the third argument?
A) A sleeper in the Cave.
B) A person in distress.
C) A blind person in a dark room.
D) A person absorbed in pleasure.

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because time awareness can exist without motion awareness, as demonstrated by the blind person in a dark room.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely experienced times when minutes felt like hours and hours like minutes. Which of the two examples—distressed or joyful—describes your reaction to trials and blessings? Are you living as if time is just a measure of your own activities, or are you aware that every passing moment is a direct gift from Him that you can never replace? Make a concrete plan to spend the next hour with conscious awareness of time as a trust from Allah.

Comparability of Duration Does Not Imply Motion
Refutes the claim that comparability of duration implies motion or corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the comparability of duration does not necessitate that the enduring entity is a body or subject to motion, as demonstrated by the example of God's essence.

Core Idea
Duration can be compared without implying the thing is a body or moving.

This passage addresses a common objection: if time involves relations to motion and admits equality and inequality, then time must be like a material essence or cause its own passing. The author refutes this by pointing to the duration of God's essence, which is comparable (e.g., a thousand years vs. a hundred) yet without implying that God is a moving body. The same logic applies to time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the gist.
Notice the author's use of an example to refute the claim.
2
Identify the objection being refuted
The passage responds to the idea that if time has relations to motion and is comparable, then time must be like a material essence or subject to passing away. Find this in the text.
The objection is implied in the first sentence.
3
Understand the counterexample
The author gives the example of God's duration: we know necessarily that a thousand years is longer than a hundred years for God. This does not make God a moving body.
Reflect on why this example is powerful for a theologian.
4
See the conclusion
The passage concludes that comparability of duration does not necessitate motion or corporeality. Thus, time's properties from motion do not make it a body.
The phrase 'فكذا ههنا' (so likewise here) applies the same reasoning to time.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this argument is important for Islamic theology: it defends the idea that God's attributes (like duration) can be discussed without anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تعرض لها نسب وإضافات إلى الحركات، فيعرض لها بسبب تلك النسب والإضافات قبولها للمساواة والمفاونة. وهذا القدر لا يوجب كون الزمان كما بالذات ، ولا يقضي عليه بكونه سبباً لا في ذاته ، منقضياً بحسب أجزاء ماهيته . والذي يحقق هذا الكلام تجسّم مادة هذا الخيال . فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن دوام ذات الله تعالى ألف سنة ، أطول من دوام ذاته مائة سنة ، ومن أنكر ذلك لم يلتفت إليه ، فثبت : أن دوام ذاته قابل للمساواة واللامساواة لا يلزم منه كون ذاته جسماً متحركاً . فكذا ههنا .
Then relations and additions to motions occur to it, so due to those relations and additions, it becomes susceptible to equality and inequality. And this much does not necessitate that time be like material essence, nor does it judge it to be a cause in its essence, passing away according to the parts of its quiddity. And what verifies this speech is the embodiment of the matter of this imagination. For we know necessarily that the duration of the essence of Allah the Exalted for a thousand years is longer than the duration of His essence for a hundred years, and whoever denies that is not paid attention to. So it is established that the duration of His essence is susceptible to equality and inequality, and it does not necessitate from that that His essence be a moving body. So likewise here.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a response to an argument that time is a concomitant of motion. It begins by stating that relations and additions to motion can give rise to susceptibility to equality and inequality. However, this does not force time to be like a material essence (كمالذات) or cause its annihilation according to parts of its quiddity. To verify this, the author gives an example: the duration of God's essence for a thousand years is longer than for a hundred years. This comparability does not imply that God's essence is a moving body. The same logic applies to time: its attributes from motion do not make it a body.

Correction: The passage shows that God's essence endures and its duration can be compared without implying God is a body or moving. Therefore, comparability of duration does not entail corporeality or motion.

Refutation of the Third Proof: Time as a Concomitant of Motion
Presents and refutes the third proof that time is a concomitant of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that time is a concomitant of motion is refuted by showing that time perception can occur without motion perception.

Core Idea
Time perception does not depend on motion perception.

This passage presents a key objection to the view that time is a concomitant of motion. The opponent argues that because perception of time correlates with perception of motion, time must depend on motion. The refutation uses counterexamples to break this correlation and shows that the causal link is not necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first the opponent's proof, then the refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that the perception of time is proportional to the perception of motion. They give examples: the distressed person (aware of motion) feels time long, the delighted person (heedless) feels it short, and sleepers lose all time perception due to lack of motion.
This is a correlation argument used to prove that time is a concomitant of motion.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation has two parts. First, sleep prevents all perception, so the sleeper's lack of time perception is not specifically due to lack of motion. Second, the blind person in a dark house, who has no motion perception, still perceives the passage of time.
These counterexamples show that time perception can exist without motion perception.
4
Reflect on the implication
The refutation weakens the argument that time is ontologically dependent on motion. The correlation between motion perception and time perception does not prove a necessary causal link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لهم : قالوا : الدليل على أن الزمان من لواحق الحركة : أنه كلما كان الشعور بالحركة أتم ، كان الشعور بالزمان أتم . ولهذا السبب فإن المغتم بمرور الزمان قد يستطيله ، والمستغرق بالطرب قد يستقصره . لأجل أن الأول شاعر بالحركات ، والثاني غافل عنها . وأما في حق النائم فهو مثل ما بما يذكر في قصة أصحاب الكهف، فإنه لما لم يكن لهم شعور بالحركة ، لا جرم لم يكن لهم شعور بالزمان . فالجواب : إن النائم إنما لم يشعر بالزمان ، لأن النوم مانع من الشعور مطلقاً ، لا لما قالوه من أن عدم شعوره بالحركة ، اقتضي عدم شعوره بالزمان . ثم نقول : هذا بالعكس أولى ، فإنا بينا : أن الأعمى الجالس في البيت المظلم الذي لا يحس بشيء من الحركات، فإنه يكون شاعراً بمرور المدة والزمان
The third proof for them: They said: The evidence that time is a concomitant of motion is that whenever the perception of motion is more complete, the perception of time is more complete. And for this reason, the person distressed by the passage of time may find it long, and the person absorbed in delight may find it short. Because the first is aware of motions, and the second is heedless of them. And as for the sleeper, it is like what is mentioned in the story of the Companions of the Cave, for since they had no perception of motion, necessarily they had no perception of time. The answer: Indeed the sleeper did not perceive time only because sleep prevents perception absolutely, not because of what they said that his lack of perception of motion necessitated his lack of perception of time. Then we say: This is rather the opposite more appropriate, for we have shown that the blind person sitting in a dark house who does not sense any motion, he is nonetheless aware of the passage of duration and time.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that time is a concomitant of motion and its refutation. The proof argues that perception of time correlates with perception of motion: the distressed person (aware of motion) feels time long, the delighted person (heedless of motion) feels it short, and sleepers (no motion perception) lose time perception entirely, as in the story of the Companions of the Cave. The refutation first points out that sleep prevents all perception, not just motion perception, so the lack of time perception is due to sleep, not lack of motion. Moreover, the blind person in a dark house, who has no motion perception, still perceives the passage of time. This disproves the claim that time perception depends on motion perception.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Counterexamples show that time perception can occur without motion perception, so correlation does not imply causation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Dilemma of Dahr: Fixed or Changing?
Razi presents a logical dilemma about the nature of dahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Razi poses about the nature of dahr (eternal time) and its causal role.

Core Idea
Can a fixed cause produce changing effects? That is the dilemma of dahr.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a sharp objection against those who define dahr as the relation of the fixed to the changing. He forces a choice: either dahr is fixed or changing, and both lead to problems. Let's examine the two horns of the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how Razi sets up a dilemma by asking whether dahr is fixed or changing.
2
Analyze the first horn
If dahr is fixed, Razi asks whether it can cause changing relations. Follow his reasoning.
He brings in Plato's view as an alternative.
3
Analyze the second horn
If dahr is changing, can it cause relations to fixed things? If so, why not just use zaman?
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Consider why Razi's objection is a strong challenge to the definition of dahr.
The answer will come in the next lesson.

السؤال الثاني : أن يقال : إنك زعمت : أن نسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر. فنقول : هذا الذي سميته بالدهر ، هل هو في نفسه ثابت مستقر ، أو هو في نفسه متغير سيال ؟ فإن كان ثابتاً فالثابت هل يجوز جعله سبباً لحصول النسب المتغيرة أو لا يجوز ؟ فإن جاز فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : المقتضى لحصول نسب بعض المتغيرات إلى بعض شيء ثابت في ذاته ، كما هو قول أفلاطون ، وإن لم يجز هذا ، فكيف جعلتم هذا الشيء المسمى بالدهر مع كونه ثابتاً سبباً لحصول النسب الواقعة بين الأشياء الثابتة، وبين الأشياء المتغيرة ؟ وذلك لان هذه النسب تكون متغيرة لا محالة فإذا كان الدهر ثابتاً ، والثابت لا يجوز جعله سبباً للنسب المتغيرة ، لزم امتناع كون الدهر سبباً لحصول الأشياء المتغيرة ؟ وذلك لأن هذه النسب تكون متغيرة لا محالة فإذا كان الدهر ثابتاً، والثابت لا يجوز جعله سبباً للنسب المتغيرة، لزم امتناع كون الدهر سبباً لحصول هذه النسب المتغيرة ، وأما ان المسمى بالدهر أمراً متغيراً في ذاته ، فالشيء المتغير في ذاته ، هل يمكن جعله سبباً لحصول النسبة مع الأشياء الثابتة ، أو لا يمكن ؟ فإن أمكن فلم لا يكون الزمان كافياً في ذلك حتى لا يحتاج إلى إثبات هذا الشيء المسمى بالدهر ، وإن لم يمكن فكيف جعلتم الدهر المتغير في ذاته ، سبباً لحصول النسبة إلى الأشياء الثابتة ؟ قال الشيخ في كتاب عيون الحكمة : ( الدهر في ذاته من السرمد ، وهو بالقياس إلى الزمان دهر ، وأقول : معناه : إن الدهر في ذاته شيء ثابت غير متغير إلا أنه إذا نسب إلى الزمان الذي هو موجود متغير في ذاته ، سمي دهراً . وهذا تصريح بأن الدهر ثابت في نفسه وذاته، إلا أنه مع كونه كذلك ، فإنه يقتضي حصول هذه النسب المتغيرة . وفي هذا اعتراف بأن الشيء قد يكون ثابتاً في ذاته ، ومع ذلك فإنه يقتضي تقدير الأحوال المتغيرة بالمقادير المخصوصة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن هذا عين مذهب أفلاطون ، وهو أن الزمان جوهر قائم بنفسه مستقل بذاته ، إلا أنه يقتضي تقدير هذه الأحوال المختلفة ، وقد ثبت أن الناصرين لمذهب أرسطاطاليس في أن الزمان مقدار الحركة ، لا يمكنهم التوغل في شيء من مضايق المباحث المتعلقة بالزمان ، إلا عند الرجوع إلى قول أفلاطون .
The second question: It is said: You claimed that the relation of the fixed to the changing is time (ad-dahr). We say: This thing which you have named 'dahr' — is it in itself fixed and stable, or is it in itself changing and flowing? If it is fixed, then the fixed — is it permissible to make it a cause for the occurrence of changing relations or not? If it is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say: The requirer for the occurrence of the relations of some changing things to others is something fixed in itself, as is the statement of Plato? And if this is not permissible, then how have you made this thing named 'dahr', despite being fixed, a cause for the occurrence of the relations occurring between fixed things and changing things? That is because these relations are necessarily changing. So if dahr is fixed, and the fixed is not permissible to make it a cause for changing relations, then the impossibility of dahr being a cause for the occurrence of the changing things necessarily follows. That is because these relations are necessarily changing. So if dahr is fixed, and the fixed is not permissible to make it a cause for changing relations, then the impossibility of dahr being a cause for the occurrence of these changing relations necessarily follows. But if what is named 'dahr' is something changing in itself, then the thing changing in itself — can it be made a cause for the occurrence of relation with fixed things, or can it not? If it is possible, then why is time (az-zaman) not sufficient for that, so that there is no need to prove this thing named 'dahr'? And if it is not possible, how did you make dahr, changing in itself, a cause for the occurrence of relation to fixed things? The Shaykh said in the book 'Uyun al-Hikma: (ad-dahr in itself is part of sarmad, and in relation to time (az-zaman) it is dahr. And I say: Its meaning is that ad-dahr in itself is something fixed, unchanging, except that when it is related to time (az-zaman) which is a changing existent in itself, it is called dahr. And this is an explicit statement that ad-dahr is fixed in itself and in its essence, except that despite being so, it necessitates the occurrence of these changing relations. And in this is an acknowledgment that a thing may be fixed in itself, and yet with that it necessitates the estimation of changing states by specific measures. If you have understood this, we say: This is exactly the doctrine of Plato, namely that time (az-zaman) is a substance subsisting by itself, independent in its essence, except that it necessitates the estimation of these different states, and it has been established that the supporters of the doctrine of Aristotle that time is the measure of motion cannot penetrate any of the straitened investigations related to time except by recourse to the statement of Plato.)
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection against a certain view about dahr (time as an eternal dimension) and then answers it using a quotation from Ibn Sina's 'Uyun al-Hikma and the author's own commentary. The objection proceeds as a dilemma: either dahr is fixed or changing. If fixed, can it cause changing relations? The objector argues that a fixed cause cannot produce changing effects, so either it is impossible or one might as well adopt Plato's view of a fixed entity. If dahr is changing, then time (zaman) might suffice without needing dahr. The answer from Ibn Sina clarifies that dahr is fixed in itself but when related to changing time it is called dahr, and it necessitates changing relations despite its fixity. The author then concludes that this is exactly Plato's view, implying that the dilemma is resolved by Plato's conception of time as an independent substance. The passage shows that even Aristotelians ultimately rely on Plato's ideas.

ثابت
متغير
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dahr must be either fixed or changing and both options work. Razi shows both lead to problems.
Correction: The dilemma reveals that the definition of dahr as 'relation of fixed to changing' is problematic without further clarification.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's objection, if dahr is fixed, what problem arises?
A) It cannot be a cause for changing relations.
B) It becomes identical to zaman.
C) It must be a substance.
D) It is the measure of motion.

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma challenges the coherence of defining dahr as the relation of fixed to changing, by showing that both a fixed and a changing dahr lead to logical difficulties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your relationship with the unchanging Creator, do you unconsciously imagine a 'fixed container' of time that holds both Him and your changing life? How does Razi's dilemma expose the incoherence of that mental picture, and what does that demand from your heart right now?
?
What if your deepest assumptions about time are built on a logical contradiction? Will you examine your own concept of time today, or will you keep using it without question, knowing Razi has shown both options lead to collapse?
?
Razi's dilemma challenges you to choose: either abandon the idea of dahr altogether or accept that your understanding of how the eternal relates to the temporal is flawed. Which will you do, and how will this change the way you read the Quran or perform your prayers starting tonight?

Ibn Sina's Resolution: Dahr Fixed in Essence, Related to Change
Ibn Sina defines dahr as fixed in itself but related to changing zaman, resolving the dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina resolves the dilemma by defining dahr as fixed in essence but related to changing time.

Core Idea
Dahr is fixed in itself yet necessitates changing relations.

Razi's dilemma left the concept of dahr in tension. Ibn Sina provides a resolution by distinguishing between dahr in itself and dahr in relation to time. This lesson explores his definition and its implications, including the surprising conclusion that it aligns with Plato's view.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's statement
Focus on the quote from 'Uyun al-Hikma and the author's explanation.
2
Understand the distinction
Dahr in itself is fixed; relative to zaman it is called dahr. This resolves the dilemma.
Notice how fixity can necessitate change.
3
See the conclusion
The author notes this is exactly Plato's doctrine, and even Aristotelians rely on it.
This shows the power of the resolution.

Priority by Essence and Causality Cannot Become Posterior
Distinguishes priority by essence/causality from temporal priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between different types of priority and why only temporal priority can reverse.

Core Idea
Temporal priority can reverse, but priority by essence or causality never does.

This lesson introduces the sixth property: the distinction between priority by essence/causality and priority by time. The author explains that while priority by essence and causality are irreversible, temporal priority can shift. Understanding this helps clarify the nature of causal and temporal relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three types of priority mentioned: by essence, by causality, and by time.
The passage is concise; each sentence adds a key point.
2
Compare the categories
Identify the difference between priority by essence/causality and priority by time. Ask yourself: why can't essential priority reverse?
Think about the father-son example as an illustration.
3
Reflect on the example
Work through the father-son example: the father is temporally prior, but after death he becomes posterior. Why is this only possible for time?
Consider the logic of causality vs. chronology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية السادسة: إن المتقدم بالذات وبالعلية لا يصير متأخراً البتة وبالعكس. أما المتقدم بالزمان فإنه قد يصير هو بعينه متأخراً بالزمان، كالأب فإنه متقدم على الابن بالزمان، ثم إنه قد يبقى بعد موته فيصير متأخراً عنه بالزمان. أما المتأخر بالزمان فإنه يمتنع أن يصير متقدماً بالزمان على ما كان متقدماً عليه. وذلك ظاهر.
The sixth property: That which is prior by essence and by causality never becomes posterior, and vice versa. As for that which is prior by time, it may become exactly posterior by time, like the father: he is prior to the son in time, but he may remain after his death and thus become posterior to him in time. As for that which is posterior by time, it cannot become prior by time to that which was prior to it. That is evident.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth property: a distinction between different types of priority. Priority by essence (e.g., the priority of the cause over the effect) and priority by causality cannot be reversed; if something is prior by essence, it cannot become posterior. However, priority by time can change. The author gives the example of a father and son: the father is prior in time to the son, but if the father dies later, the son may outlive him, making the father posterior in time to the son. The reverse—something posterior in time becoming prior—is impossible. This highlights the flexibility of temporal priority.

التقدم بالعلية
التقدم بالزمان
التأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of priority are the same and irreversible.
Correction: Only priority by essence and causality are irreversible; temporal priority can reverse.

Checkpoint

A) Priority by essence
B) Priority by causality
C) Priority by time
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Priority by essence and causality is fixed, but temporal priority can change.

?

?
In your relationship with Allah, what is truly prior by essence – such as tawheed – and are you giving it the fixed priority it deserves, or are you allowing temporal distractions to shift it?

Seventh Property: Causal and Temporal Priority Are Mutually Exclusive
Argues that causal and temporal priority are mutually exclusive, but natural priority can combine with temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why causal priority and temporal priority cannot coincide, and how natural priority differs.

Core Idea
A complete cause requires immediate effect; thus causal priority excludes temporal delay.

This lesson examines the seventh property: the relationship between causal priority and temporal priority. The author argues that a complete cause, by definition, produces its effect without delay, so if there is a time gap, the cause is not truly complete. However, priority by nature can coexist with temporal priority, as illustrated by the example of one being prior to two.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument structure become clear.
Note the use of 'because' to introduce the reasoning.
2
Understand the definition of complete cause
The complete cause (al-illa al-tāmma) is defined as that which cannot be separated from its effect. If the effect is delayed, the cause is not complete.
This is key to the entire argument.
3
Follow the incompatibility argument
From the definition, if there is a temporal delay, the cause is not complete, so causal priority (which requires completeness) cannot coexist with temporal priority.
The conclusion is that they never coincide.
4
Note the exception for natural priority
Priority by nature (like one being prior to two) can coincide with temporal priority because it does not involve the same necessity as causality.
The example clarifies the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية السابعة: أن نقول: التقدم بالعلية والتقدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان البتة، لأنه لما ثبت أن العلة التامة في جميع الأمور المعتبرة في العلية يمتنع تخلف المعلول عنها، لزم أن يقال: إنه حيث حصل التخلف لم تكن العلة علة تامة في العلية. فيثبت: أن التقدم بالعلية والتقدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان البتة. أما المتقدم بالطبع مع التقدم بالزمان فقد يجتمعان، فإن الواحد متقدم على الاثنين بالطبع، وقد يكون متقدماً عليه أيضاً في الزمان.
The seventh property: We say: Priority by causality and priority by time never coincide. Because when it is established that the complete cause (al-illa al-tāmma) in all matters considered in causality prohibits the effect from being delayed from it, it follows that wherever there is delay, the cause is not a complete cause in causality. So it is established that priority by causality and priority by time never coincide. As for priority by nature along with priority by time, they may coincide, for the one is prior to the two by nature, and it may also be prior to it in time.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage discusses the seventh property: the incompatibility of causal priority and temporal priority. The reasoning is based on the definition of the complete cause (al-illa al-tāmma): if the cause is complete, the effect cannot be delayed; it occurs immediately. Therefore, if there is a temporal delay between cause and effect, the cause cannot be a complete cause. Consequently, priority by causality (where the cause is prior in causality) and priority by time (where one thing occurs before another in time) are mutually exclusive. However, priority by nature (like the priority of one over two) can coexist with temporal priority; the one can be both naturally and temporally prior to the two.

Key Terms
التقدم بالعلية
التقدم بالزمان
التقدم بالطبع
العلة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a cause must always be temporally prior to its effect, as in common experience.
Correction: This property shows that for a complete cause, temporal priority is not necessary; the effect is simultaneous with the cause. Temporal priority only applies when the cause is not complete.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh property, can priority by causality and priority by time ever coincide?
A) Yes, always
B) Yes, sometimes
C) No, never
D) Only in special cases

Key Takeaway
Causal priority and temporal priority are mutually exclusive because a complete cause necessitates its effect without delay, whereas natural priority can coincide with temporal priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for something and it does not happen immediately, do you assume your prayer was not a complete cause? How does this lesson challenge you to re-examine your understanding of God's role as the sole immediate cause of all effects?
?
You often think of your own actions as causes that produce results after some time. How does the truth that a complete cause excludes temporal delay force you to reconsider the true nature of causality and your dependence on God?
?
If causal priority and temporal priority never coincide, then what you experience as 'cause and effect' in daily life are not genuine causes in the complete sense. How does this realization demand that you change the way you attribute power and effectiveness to yourself versus to God?

Property Seven: Causal and Temporal Priority Cannot Coexist
Razi explains why causal and temporal priority cannot coexist, but natural and temporal can.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why causal priority and temporal priority are mutually exclusive, and why natural priority can coexist with temporal priority.

Core Idea
A complete cause and its effect are simultaneous; time delay indicates an incomplete cause.

This lesson examines the seventh property from Razi's discussion on priority. Razi argues that causal priority and temporal priority cannot both apply to the same pair of things, because a complete cause necessitates its effect without delay. However, natural priority (like the one being prior to two by nature) can coexist with temporal priority. Let's delve into the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the clear distinction between the main claim and the exception.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries a logical step.
2
Understand the concept of a complete cause
Razi defines a complete cause as one that necessitates its effect without any delay. Grasp why this definition is crucial for the argument.
An incomplete cause may have its effect delayed, but a complete cause cannot.
3
Follow the proof of incompatibility
The proof asserts: since a complete cause cannot have its effect delayed, if there is a temporal delay, the cause is not complete. Thus, something cannot be both causally prior (as a complete cause) and temporally prior to the same thing.
The reasoning is straightforward but foundational for causality in kalam.
4
Examine the exception for natural priority
Natural priority (like the one being prior to two by nature) can coexist with temporal priority. Razi gives the example of the number one being prior to two both by nature and possibly in time.
This shows that not all types of priority are mutually exclusive with temporal priority.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this property shapes the understanding of causation: a cause does not necessarily temporally precede its effect; rather, causation implies simultaneous dependence.
This refines the concept of causality in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخاصية السابعة : أن نقول التقدم بالعلية والتقدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان البتة ، لأنه لما ثبت أن العلة التامة في جميع الأمور المعتبرة في العلية ، يمتنع تخلف المعلول عنها ، لزم أن يقال : إنه حيث حصل التخلف لم تكن العلة علة تامة في العلية. فيثبت : أن التقدم بالعلية ، والتقدم بالزمان لا يجتمعان البتة . أما المتقدم بالطبع مع التقدم بالزمان ، فقد يجتمعان فإن الواحد متقدم على الاثنين بالطبع ، وقد يكون متقدماً عليه أيضاً في الزمان
The seventh property: That we say: Priority by causation and priority by time cannot coexist at all, because when it is established that the complete cause in all matters considered in causation cannot have its effect delayed, it necessarily follows that wherever there is delay, the cause was not a complete cause in causation. So it is established that priority by causation and priority by time cannot coexist at all. As for priority by nature together with priority by time, they may coexist: the one is prior to the two by nature, and it may also be prior to it in time.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage states the seventh property: causal priority and temporal priority are mutually exclusive. The reasoning is that a complete cause (al-illa al-tamma) necessitates its effect without delay; if there is a temporal delay, the cause is not complete. Therefore, something cannot be both causally prior and temporally prior to the same thing. However, natural priority (al-taqaddum bi al-tab') and temporal priority can coexist: for example, the number one is naturally prior to two and can also be temporally prior if it exists before two.

Key Terms
تقدم بالعلية
تقدم بالزمان
علة تامة
تخلف المعلول
تقدم بالطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a cause always precedes its effect in time.
Correction: This property shows that a complete cause and its effect are simultaneous; temporal priority implies the cause is not complete. Causal priority does not entail temporal priority.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh property, can something be both causally prior and temporally prior to the same thing?
A) Yes, always
B) No, never
C) Only if it is also naturally prior
D) Only if the cause is incomplete

Key Takeaway
A complete cause and its effect are simultaneous; causal priority excludes temporal priority, but natural priority can coexist with temporal priority.

How would your worship change if you believed that your sincere act is already its own effect, simultaneous with your intention?
